Travel as an Excuse to Terminate a Lease: Hanafi Verification Procedure
Procedure for verifying travel as an excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for verifying travel as an excuse to terminate an ijarah and distinguish valid from invalid excuse scenarios.

Core Idea
Intent verified by oath or witnesses: not all travel excuses lease termination.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq details the procedure a judge follows when a lessee claims travel as an excuse to void an ijarah contract. The Hanafi school recognizes genuine travel as a valid excuse, but it must be proven through either witness testimony or the lessee's oath. The passage also clarifies several concrete scenarios, establishing what does and does not qualify as a valid excuse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that travel can be a legitimate excuse (udhr) to terminate a lease in the Hanafi school, but it requires verification.
The burden is on the lessee to prove genuine intent.
2
Outline the verification procedure
The judge first asks the lessee who his travel companions are, then questions them. If they confirm the lessee's preparation and intent, the excuse is established. If they deny, the judge administers an oath to the lessee.
The oath is sworn by Allah on the lessee's intention.
3
Note the authorities
Al-Karkhi and al-Quduri endorsed the oath procedure. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
4
Apply to the given scenarios
The passage lists: buying a house to move into is not an excuse; finding a cheaper journey is not an excuse; buying camels for travel is an excuse. Understand the reasoning: genuine change of residence vs. mere improvement.
The distinction hinges on whether the lessee is compelled to change his residence.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi
The oath procedure is relied upon. If the lessee affirms under oath, the excuse is accepted even without witnesses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للمستأجر . مع من تسافر؛ فإن قال فلان وفلان فالقاضي يسألهما هل يخرج معكم المستأجر مع وهل استعد للسفر، فإن قالا نعم ثبت العذر، وإن قالا لا فإن القاضي يحلف المستأجر بالله إنك عزمت على السفر، وإليه مال الكرخي والقدوري فلو خرج من المصر ثم عاد يحلف بالله قد خرجت قاصداً للسفر الذي ذكرت كذا في الخلاصة وغيرها. وفي الخلاصة : فإن لم يترك السفر ولكن وجد أرخص منها فهذا ليس بعذر ولو اشترى منزلاً وأراد التحول فيها فهذا ليس بعذر، ولو اشترى إبلاً فهو عذر
For the lessee: With whom are you traveling? If he says so-and-so, the judge asks them: Will the lessee go out with you? And has he prepared for travel? If they say yes, the excuse is established. If they say no, the judge swears the lessee by Allah: You resolved to travel. Al-Karkhi and al-Quduri inclined to this. If he left the city then returned, he swears by Allah: I went out intending the travel you mentioned. Thus in al-Khulasa and others. In al-Khulasa: if he did not abandon the travel but found a cheaper one, this is not an excuse. If he bought a house and wants to move into it, this is not an excuse. If he bought camels, it is an excuse.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage rules on when a tenant's travel is a valid excuse to terminate an ijarah contract. The judge verifies by asking the travel companions; if they confirm the tenant's intent and preparation, the excuse is established. If they deny, the tenant is put on oath. The passage clarifies that merely finding a cheaper journey or buying a house is not an excuse, but buying camels for travel is.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A legitimate excuse recognized by shari'a that permits voiding a necessary contract.
فسخ الإجارة (faskh al-ijarah): Termination of a lease contract before its term.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The lease is voided immediately
B) The judge swears the tenant on his intent
C) The tenant must continue the lease
D) The judge appoints new companions

Key Takeaway
Travel is a valid excuse to void an ijarah only if genuine intent is proven by witness or oath.

Direct vs. Indirect Causation in Damage Liability
Distinction between direct and indirect causation in damage liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct and indirect causation (mubashir vs. sabab) and determine when transgression (ta'addi) is required for liability in Hanafi daman.

Core Idea
Direct causation always demands liability; indirect causation requires transgression.

This lesson explores a classic Hanafi ruling on liability for fire and water damage from leased property. The core principle is not the act itself but the nature of causation: whether it is direct (mubashir) or indirect (sabab). Indirect causation only creates liability if the act involved transgression (ta'addi). We will examine why burning stubble on one's own land with calm wind does not lead to liability, while excessive irrigation does, and how this distinction applies across various scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Read the passage to determine what action is being ruled upon (burning stubble on leased/borrowed land) and the initial ruling (no liability).
2
Classify the causation type
Determine whether the act is direct causation (mubashir) or indirect causation (sabab). Burning stubble that spreads by wind is indirect; sparks flying from a blacksmith's hammer are direct.
Direct: actor directly causes harm without intermediary. Indirect: harm occurs through a separate cause (e.g., wind).
3
Apply the liability rule
Apply the Hanafi rule: if direct, liability attaches even without transgression; if indirect, liability requires transgression (ta'addi). Check whether the act involved transgression.
Transgression means acting beyond permissible bounds, such as excessive fire, turbulent wind, or placing a coal on the road.
4
Evaluate exceptions and details
Examine the nuances: calm vs. turbulent wind, excessive water vs. fire, and right of passage. These determine whether the act remains permissible or becomes transgressive.
Water naturally flows, so excess is transgressive; fire naturally goes out, so only excessive fire is transgressive.
5
Synthesize the principle
Formulate the general principle: in Hanafi law, indirect causation (sabab) without transgression does not create liability, while direct causation (mubashir) always does. This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the underlying qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولو أحرق حصائد أرض مستأجرة أو مستعارة فاحترق شيء في أرض غيره لم يضمنه) حصد الزرع جزه والحصائد جمع حصيدة وحصيدوهما الزرع المحصود والمراد هنا ما يبقى من أصل الزرع في الأرض، ولا يخفى أن هذه المسألة حقها أن تذكر في الجنايات ولهذا ذكر في الهداية مسائل منثورة. وإنما لم يضمن لأن هذه الأشياء تسبب وشرط الضمان التعدي ولم يوجد فصار كما لو حفر بئراً في ملك نفسه فتلف به إنسان بخلاف ما إذا رمى سهماً في ملكه فأصاب إنساناً حيث يضمن لأنه مباشر فلا يشترط فيه التعدي لأن المباشرة علة فلا يبطل حكمها بعذر والسبب ليس بعلة فلا بد من التعدي ليلتحق بالعلة وإحراق الحصائد في مثله مباح فلا يضاف التلف إليه . قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : هذا إذا كانت الرياح غير مضطربة، فلو كانت مضطربة يضمن يعلم أنها لا تستقر فلا يعذر فيضمن وفي الخانية : لو كانت الريح غير ساكنة يضمن استحساناً. وذكر في النهاية معزياً إلى التمرتاشي : لو وضع جمرة في الطريق فأحرقت شيئاً ضمن لأنه متعد بالوضع ولو رفعته الريح إلى شيء فأحرقته لا يضمن لأن الريح نسخت فعله، ولو أخرج الحداد الحديد من النار في مكانه فوضعه على ما يطرق عليه وضربه بالمطرقة وخرج شرار النار إلى طريق العامة وأحرق شيئاً ،ضمن ولو لم يضربه ولكن أخرج الريح شيئاً فأحرق شيئاً لم يضمن. ولو سقى أرضه سقياً لا تحتمله الأرض فتعدى إلى أرض غيره ضمن منتفعاً بما فعله متعدياً فقال خواهر زاده وشمس الأئمة السرخسي : إذا أوقد لأنه لم يكن ناراً عظيماً في أرضه بحيث لا تحتمله وتعدى إلى زرع غيره وأفسده يضمن لا محالة اهـ. وفي السفينة فرق أصحابنا بين الماء والنار فقال : إذا أوقد ناراً عظيمة في أرض نفسه فتعدى فأحرق شيئاً لا يضمن لأن النار من شأنها الخمود بخلاف ما إذا ملأ أرضه ماء بحيث لا تحتمله فإنه يضمن لأن الماء شأنه السيلان. وفي فتاوى أهل رقند : أوقد في التنور ناراً لا يحتمله فأحرق بيته وتعدى إلى بيت جاره فأحرقه ضمن وفي فتاوى الفضلي : رجل يمر في . ملكه أو في ملك غيره بنار فوقعت شرارة من ناره على ثوب إنسان فأحرقته ضمن. وفي النوادر عن أبي يوسف : إن من مر بالنار في موضع له المرور فهبت الريح فأوقعت شرارة في مال إنسان لا يضمن، وإن مر بها في موضع ليس له حق المرور ينظر؛ إن هبت بها الريح لا يضمن، وإن وقعت منه شرارة ضمن
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he burns harvested crops on leased or borrowed land and something catches fire on another's land, he is not liable.) Harvested means cut; 'hasaid' is plural for harvested crop, and what is meant here is the stubble left in the ground. It is not hidden that this matter should be mentioned in the chapter of transgressions, so al-Hidayah mentioned dispersed issues. The reason he is not liable is because these are causation (tasabbub) and the condition for liability is transgression (ta'addi), which is absent. It is like someone who digs a well on his own property and a person falls into it, but different from shooting an arrow on his property and hitting someone, where he is liable because it is direct action. For direct action, transgression is not required; for causation, it is. Burning stubble is permitted in such circumstances, so the damage is not attributed to him. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: This is if the winds are calm; if they are turbulent, he is liable because he knows they will not settle. In al-Khaniyya: if the wind is not still, he is liable by istihsan. In al-Nihaya citing al-Tamartashi: if he places a coal on the road and it burns something, he is liable because he transgressed by placing it; if the wind lifts it and burns, he is not liable because the wind nullified his action. If a blacksmith takes iron from the fire, places it on an anvil, and strikes it, and sparks fly to the public road and burn something, he is liable; if he does not strike but the wind carries it, he is not liable. If he irrigates his land excessively so that water overflows to another's land, he is liable. Khwahar Zadeh and Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: If he lights a great fire on his land that cannot be contained, and it spreads to another's crop and damages it, he is liable without question. In al-Safina, our scholars distinguished between water and fire: if he lights a great fire on his land and it spreads and burns, he is not liable because fire tends to die out; but if he fills his land with water that cannot be contained, he is liable because water tends to flow. In Fatawa of Ahl-i-Raqand: if he lights a fire in an oven that cannot contain it, and it burns his house and spreads to his neighbor's house, he is liable. In Fatawa al-Fadli: a man passes by with fire on his property or another's, and a spark falls on a garment and burns it, he is liable. In al-Nawadir from Abu Yusuf: if someone passes with fire in a place where he has the right to pass, and the wind carries a spark to someone's property, he is not liable; if he passes in a place where he has no right, details: if wind carries, no liability; if a spark falls from him, he is liable.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage elaborates the Hanafi doctrine of causation. The key distinction is between direct action (mubashir) and indirect causation (sabab). Direct action creates liability without transgression; causation requires transgression. Burning stubble on one's own land is permissible, so no liability. But if the wind is turbulent or the fire is excessive, the act becomes transgressive. Water and fire are treated differently: water naturally flows, so excess causes liability; fire naturally dies, so no liability unless intentionally excessive. Various scenarios illustrate the principle.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubashir): Direct agent causing harm without intermediate cause.
سبب (sabab): Indirect cause that requires transgression to create liability.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; exceeding permissible bounds in an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a person not liable when burning stubble on his own land and it spreads to another's?
A) Because fire is always permissible
B) Because it is causation without transgression
C) Because the wind is always blamed
D) Because the other person's land is also leased

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, indirect causation without transgression does not create liability, but direct causation always does.

Fuller Beating Clothes on Another's Land Not Liable
Ruling on non-liability of fuller using another's land

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a fuller beating clothes on another's land and explain the role of custom (urf) in determining liability.

Core Idea
A fuller is not liable for beating clothes on another's land because custom implies implicit permission.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a concise ruling that highlights the influence of custom (urf) in Hanafi law. The fuller's action is considered customary and not transgressive, so he bears no liability. Understanding this principle helps clarify when property rights are waived by social practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is beating clothes on another's land without explicit permission. The ruling is 'la yadman' (not liable).
2
Identify the legal basis
Custom (urf) implies that such actions are tolerated; no express permission is needed for customary use of open land.
Hanafi law gives weight to urf as a source of fiqh.
3
Distinguish from transgressive acts
If the fuller causes damage beyond what is customary (e.g., digging holes or damaging the land), he would be liable because his action would then be considered transgression (ta'addi).
4
Relate to ijara (hire)
The fuller is often hired; this ruling clarifies that using the landowner's space during the work does not create additional liability beyond the contract.
The ruling protects workers in customary trades.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتمة سألت والدي عن القصار يدق الثياب في أرض غيره لم يضمنه
In al-Tatimma: I asked my father about the fuller who beats clothes on another's land; he said he is not liable.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This brief ruling states that a fuller (one who beats clothes to clean them) is not liable for damage to the clothes or the land if he beats them on another's property. The implicit reason is that his action is customary and not transgressive; the landowner's right is not violated in a way that causes liability for the fuller's own goods.

Key Terms
القصار (al-qassar): The fuller: a person who beats clothes to clean them.
يدق (yadqqu): He beats (clothes) as a method of cleaning.
لا يضمن (la yadman): He is not liable; no compensation required.

Tailor and Dyer Partnership by Istihsan
Ruling on partner seated in shop on half share

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a craftsman seating a partner in his shop on half share by istihsan, and distinguish this from the qiyas ruling.

Core Idea
A partnership of crafts is valid by istihsan because custom eliminates ambiguity, even though qiyas would reject it.

This passage addresses a common commercial arrangement where a tailor or dyer invites another person to accept work in his shop on a half-share basis. The Hanafi school rules this valid by istihsan (juristic preference) due to widespread custom, overriding the strict analogy (qiyas) that would deem it invalid. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and explores its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
The text rules the arrangement 'sahih' (valid) by istihsan, contrasting with qiyas which would reject it.
2
Understand why qiyas rejects it
Qiyas would compare it to an invalid contract due to gharar (uncertainty) or to a lease with unknown wage. The arrangement involves unknown future work, making it problematic under strict analogy.
Gharar arises because the amount of work and profit is unknown at contract formation.
3
Explore the istihsan justification
The arrangement is classified as shirkat al-sanai' (partnership of crafts), not ijarah or shirkat al-wujuh. In a partnership of crafts, both parties share work and profit. Custom (urf) makes it acceptable.
The commentator clarifies that it is not shirkat al-wujuh, which involves buying and selling on credit.
4
Examine the implied partnership in acceptance
The commentator argues that partnership in the result implies partnership in acceptance, so the acceptance of work is shared even if one party physically collects the work.
This reasoning is derived from al-Ghiyathiyya and the response to its objection.
5
Apply to similar examples
The passage gives examples of cows, chickens, silkworms, and debt collection to illustrate the principle that when one party provides an asset and the other works, the yield belongs to the asset owner and the worker gets fair wages, not the agreed share. This reinforces the istihsan ruling.
These examples show that the Hanafi school applies a consistent logic: ownership of the asset dictates entitlement to produce, with compensation for labor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أقعد خياط أو صباغ في حانوته من يطرح عليه العلم بالنصف صح وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يصح وحق هذه المسألة أن تذكر في كتاب الشركة، ووجه الاستحسان أن هذه شركة الصنائع وليست بإجارة لأن تفسير شركة الصنائع أن يكون العمل عليهما وإن كان أحدهما متولي العلم بحذاقته والآخر متولي القبول لوجاهته وإذا وجد ماله سبيل إلى الجواز وهو متعارف يوجب القول بصحته فيكون العمل واجباً عليهما والأجر بينهما على ما عرف في موضعه قال الشارح : وقول صاحب الهداية هذه شركة الوجوه فيه نوع إشكال فإن شركة الوجوه أن يشتركا على أن يشتريا بوجوههما ويبيعا وليس في الشركة بيع وشراء وإنما هي شركة صنائع . قال في الغياثية : شركة التقبيل هي على أن يتقبل الأعمال وهنا ليس كذلك بل هما اشتركا في الحاصل من الأجر وليست شركة صنائع. وأجبت بأن الشركة في الخارج تقتضي الشركة في التقبيل فثبت فيه اقتضاء إذ ليس في كلامهما إلا تخصيص أحدهما بالتقبل والآخر بالعمل، وتخصيص الشيء بالذكر لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه فأثبتنا الشركة في التقبل اقتضاء .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية: دفع الآخر بقرة بالعلف ليكون الخارج بينهما نصفين فالحادث كله لصاحب البقرة وعليه أجرة مثل المدفوع إليه وثمن العلف، ومثله لو دفع الدجاجة إلى آخر بالنصف، ولو دفع بذر العليق إلى امرأة بالنصف فقامت عليه حتى أدركت فالعليق لصاحب البذر وعلى صاحب البذر قيمة العلف وأجرة مثلها. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث دفع إلى امرأة دود التقوم عليها بنفقتها على أن العليق بينهما نصفان فهو بمنزلة المضارب وكل العليق لصاحب الدود وعليه أجر المثل وثمن الأوراق، ولو غصب من آخر دود القز وبيض الدجاج فأمسكه حتى خرج العليق والفرخ . قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : إن خرج بنفسه فهو لصاحبه رجل له غريم في مصر آخر فقال لرجل اذهب إليه وطالبه بالدين وإذا قبضت فلك عشرة ففعل فله أجر مثله اهـ. ولقائل أن يقول: هذه مكررة مع قوله فيما سبق وتقبل إن اشترك خياطان أو خياط وصباغ قلنا : ذكر هناك شركة لا الصنائع قصداً وهنا بين ما إذا وقع العقد على شركة الصنائع ضمناً فبهذا الاعتبار لا تكرار .
And if a tailor or dyer seats in his shop someone who collects work for him on half, it is valid. This is istihsan; the qiyas is that it is not valid. This matter should be mentioned in the book of partnership. The justification for istihsan is that this is a partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sanai'), not ijarah. The interpretation of partnership of crafts is that the work is on both, even if one manages the craft due to skill and the other manages acceptance due to reputation. Since there is a way to make it permissible and it is customary, it necessitates saying it is valid. So work is obligatory on both and the wage is between them as known in its place. The commentator said: The statement of the author of al-Hidayah that this is shirkat al-wujuh has some difficulty; shirkat al-wujuh is that they partner to buy and sell on credit, but here there is no sale or purchase. It is a partnership of crafts. In al-Ghiyathiyya: shirkat al-taqabbul is to accept contracts; here it is not that, but they both share in the resulting wage. I answered that partnership in the result implies partnership in acceptance, so it is established by implication. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: if one gives a cow to another with fodder, with the produce to be half each, then all product belongs to the owner of the cow, and he owes like wages to the recipient and the price of fodder. Similarly for a chicken. If he gives silkworm eggs to a woman to raise on half, the silk is for the owner, and he owes wages and cost of leaves. In Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: if he gives silkworms to a woman to tend with her expense, the silk is for the owner and he owes like wages and cost of leaves. If someone usurps silkworms and chicken eggs and keeps them until the silk and chicks emerge, Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: if they emerge naturally, they belong to the owner. A man had a debtor in another city; he said to a man: Go to him and demand the debt; if you collect, you get ten. He went and collected so he gets his like wage. And it may be said: This is repetitive with what was said earlier. We say: There it was shirkat al-sanai' intentionally, here it is when the contract is on partnership of crafts impliedly, so no repetition.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage deals with a common commercial arrangement: a craftsman seating a partner in his shop to accept work, splitting profit. The author declares it valid by istihsan, even though analogy would reject it due to uncertainty. It is classified as a partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sanai'), not a lease. The commentator clarifies that it resembles shirkat al-wujuh only ineffectively. Several examples from other works (cow, chicken, silkworms) show that such profit-sharing arrangements are treated as analogous to mudaraba, where the owner of the asset is entitled to the entire produce and must pay the caretaker fair wages. The final example of debt collection for a fee contradicts the general rule, giving the collector fair wages instead of the stipulated amount.

Key Terms
شركة الصنائع (shirkat al-sanai'): Partnership of craftsmen where both work and share earnings.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict analogy for a stronger reason (custom, necessity).
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
شركة الوجوه (shirkat al-wujuh): Partnership on credit where partners have no capital but rely on their reputation to buy and sell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the validity of a tailor seating someone in his shop on half share according to the Hanafi school?
A) Qiyas (analogy)
B) Ijma (consensus)
C) Istihsan (juristic preference) due to custom
D) A clear text from the Quran or Sunnah

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates partnership of crafts by istihsan due to custom, overriding strict qiyas.

Debt Collection for Fixed Fee: Fair Wage Not Agreed Amount
Ruling on compensation for debt collection service

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that an agent who collects a debt for a conditional fee is entitled to ujrat al-mithl (fair wage), not the named amount.

Core Idea
Conditional fee in debt collection is invalid; agent gets fair wage for work done.

This short ruling from the Hanafi tradition addresses a common scenario: a person promises a fixed amount to someone for collecting a debt, but only if successful. The text clarifies that the collector is not bound by that promise but instead deserves the customary wage for his effort. This illustrates a key Hanafi principle: when a fee is conditional on an event outside the agent's control, the contract is treated as a form of ju'ala, and the fee must be known or determined by custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and promise
A man (employer) tells another man (agent): 'Go to my debtor, demand the debt, and if you collect, you get ten.' The agent goes and performs the task.
The fee is conditional on successful collection, not guaranteed.
2
Determine the legal status of the promised fee
The named fee (ten) is tied to an uncertain event (collection). In Hanafi law, such a conditional fee in a contract of ju'ala or ijarah is invalid because it introduces gharar (uncertainty) into the consideration.
The fee is unenforceable as agreed.
3
Apply the rule: conditional fee → ujrat al-mithl
Since the agent performed the work (going and demanding), he is entitled to fair compensation (ujrat al-mithl) for his effort, not the named amount. The contract is valid but the fee term is replaced by custom.
The ruling is falahu ajr mithlihi (he has his like wage).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل له غريم في مصر آخر فقال لرجل اذهب إليه وطالبه بالدين وإذا قبضت فلك عشرة ففعل فله أجر مثله اهـ.
A man had a debtor in another city. He said to a man: Go to him and demand the debt; if you collect, you get ten. He did so; he has his like wage. End.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This is a short ruling: if a person hires another to collect a debt and promises a fixed amount (ten) upon success, the collector is not entitled to that amount but to the customary fair wage (ujrat al-mithl) for his effort. This is because the contract is a form of ju'ala, and the Hanafi school requires the fee to be known or determined by custom; here, the named fee is conditional on success, but the collector's effort is not conditional, so he deserves compensation equivalent to his work.

Key Terms
أجر المثل (ujrat al-mithl): Fair market wage for similar work, not the agreed amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The fixed sum of ten
B) Nothing
C) Fair wages for his effort
D) Double the sum

Key Takeaway
When a fee is promised conditionally in debt collection, the collector is entitled to the fair wage (ujrat al-mithl), not the named sum.

Hiring a Load Carrier to Mecca: Validity by Custom (Istihsan)
Ruling on validity of leasing a load carrier with unspecified but customary items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a hire contract with unspecified load by referring to custom (urf), and distinguish this from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Custom (urf) can resolve contractual uncertainty, making valid what qiyas would reject.

In this passage, the Hanafi school addresses a practical scenario: hiring a carrier for a pilgrimage journey without fully specifying what will be carried. The ruling illustrates the use of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas, and the role of custom (urf) in determining contractual obligations. The Shafi'i school disagrees, requiring full specification to avoid gharar (excessive uncertainty).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and Hanafi ruling
Carefully read the first part of the passage: a person hires a 'load carrier' (haml) to carry a litter (mahmil) and two riders to Mecca. The Hanafi ruling is that it is valid (sahih) and the hirer receives the customary litter and load.
2
Understand the qiyas (analogy) that would invalidate
The passage explicitly states that qiyas would not validate the contract due to uncertainty (jahalah) in the load. Imam al-Shafi'i follows this qiyas, making the contract invalid.
Qiyas here is based on the general prohibition of gharar in contracts.
3
See the istihsan justification
The author explains the 'wajh al-istihsan' (justification for juristic preference): the uncertainty is removed by referring to custom (al-muta'arif). The custom defines what is typically carried: provisions, cover, etc. Thus the contract is valid.
This is a key application of the Hanafi principle that custom has legal weight.
4
Note the Shafi'i counterposition and the thamarah al-khilaf
The Shafi'i school holds it invalid due to persisting gharar. The practical consequence (thamarah) is that in Hanafi practice such contracts are enforceable, whereas Shafi'is require full specification of the load.
The author also clarifies that this ruling is not repetitive of the earlier case of hiring a donkey without specifying its load – both are valid by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رحمه الله : (ولو استأجر حملاً ليحمل عليه محملا وراكبين إلى مكة صح وله المحمل المعتاد والقياس أن لا يجوز للجهالة وهو قول الإمام الشافعي ووجه الاستحسان أن هذه الجهالة تزول بالصرف إلى المتعارف وله المتعارف من الحمل والزاد والغطاء وغير ذلك مما معلوم عند أهل العرف، لا يقال هذه متكررة مع قوله «وإن استأجر حماراً ولم يسم ما يحمل»
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If he hires a load carrier to carry a litter and two riders to Mecca, it is valid, and he has the customary litter. The qiyas is that it is not valid due to uncertainty, and this is the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i. The justification for istihsan is that this uncertainty is removed by referring to custom. He has the customary load, provisions, cover, etc., known to people of custom. It cannot be said this is repetitive with his statement: 'If he hires a donkey and does not specify what to carry.')
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage rules on hiring a camel (often generically a 'load carrier') to carry a litter and two riders to Mecca. The Hanafi school validates it by istihsan despite uncertainty because the custom (urf) defines the items clearly: the litter, provisions, covering, etc. Qiyas would reject it due to gharar, and the Shafi'i school follows this. The author notes this is not repetitive with an earlier ruling about hiring a donkey without specifying the load, which is also valid by custom.

Key Terms
متعارف (muta'arif): Customary practice that removes ambiguity in contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The carrier agrees to any load
B) Custom specifies the usual items
C) The contract mentions weight
D) Both riders are named

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates otherwise uncertain contracts by referring to custom, demonstrating the operation of istihsan over qiyas.

Validity of Craftsman Partnership by Istihsan
Expanding the istihsan ruling for a shared-profit contract between a shopkeeper and a craftsman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a shared-profit contract between a shopkeeper and a craftsman, explain the istihsan reasoning, and distinguish it from an invalid hire contract.

Core Idea
Custom and need validate a partnership of crafts despite unknown wage.

In this lesson, we examine a common commercial arrangement: a shopkeeper provides space and a craftsman provides skill, sharing profits equally. The strict analogy (qiyas) would invalidate this due to uncertainty in the wage, but the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to uphold it as a valid partnership of crafts. We will explore the reasoning and its application to similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is a contract where a tailor or dyer invites another to work in his shop, with the customer cloth brought by the worker and profits halved. The matn declares it valid (sahih) by istihsan.
The ruling is from Kanz al-Daqaiq, a core Hanafi matn.
2
Locate the Proof (Dalil)
The proof is istihsan, which departs from qiyas. The commentator explains that this is a partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sana'i'), not a hire (ijarah), because both contribute: one with skill, the other with prestige (shop location).
Istihsan is a primary Hanafi source; custom ('urf) supports validity.
3
Analyze the Istihsan Reasoning (Wajh al-Dalalah)
Since the contract can be construed as a partnership, and such arrangements are customary among people, istihsan dictates validity. The work becomes obligatory on both, and the wage is distributed by custom.
The key is that the contract has a path to validity via partnership, avoiding the uncertainty of a fixed wage.
4
Address the Objection from Al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah called it a partnership of faces (shirkat al-wujuh), but the commentator clarifies it is a partnership of crafts. The objector argues no buying/selling, but the commentator replies that partnership in results implies partnership in acceptance (taqabbul).
The phrase 'specification of something by mention does not negate the ruling from what is other than it' is a usul principle.
5
Examine Supporting Cases
The commentary cites examples from al-Tatarkhaniyyah and Fatawa Abu al-Layth: sharing cow produce, chicken eggs, silkworms, and a debt collection agency. In each, the owner of the asset gets the produce but owes compensation to the worker for labor and expenses.
These cases reinforce that the arrangement is not a pure partnership but a service contract where profit-sharing is allowed by custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( ولو أقعد خياط أو صباغ في حانوته من يطرح عليه العلم بالنصف صح) وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يصح وحق هذه المسألة أن تذكر في كتاب الشركة، ووجه الاستحسان أن هذه شركة الصنائع وليست بإجارة لأن تفسير شركة الصنائع أن يكون العمل عليهما وإن كان أحدهما متولي العلم بحذاقته والآخر متولي القبول لوجاهته وإذا وجد ماله سبيل إلى الجواز وهو متعارف يوجب القول بصحته فيكون العمل واجباً عليهما والأجر بينهما على ما عرف في موضعه قال الشارح : وقول صاحب الهداية هذه شركة الوجوه فيه نوع إشكال فإن شركة الوجوه أن يشتركا على أن يشتريا بوجوههما ويبيعا وليس في الشركة بيع وشراء وإنما هي شركة صنائع . قال في الغياثية : شركة التقبيل هي على أن يتقبل الأعمال وهنا ليس كذلك بل هما اشتركا في الحاصل من الأجر وليست شركة صنائع. وأجبت بأن الشركة في الخارج تقتضي الشركة في التقبيل فثبت فيه اقتضاء إذ ليس في كلامهما إلا تخصيص أحدهما بالتقبل والآخر بالعمل، وتخصيص الشيء بالذكر لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه فأثبتنا الشركة في التقبل اقتضاء .اهـ. وفي التتارخانية: دفع الآخر بقرة بالعلف ليكون الخارج بينهما نصفين فالحادث كله لصاحب البقرة وعليه أجرة مثل المدفوع إليه وثمن العلف، ومثله لو دفع الدجاجة إلى آخر بالنصف، ولو دفع بذر العليق إلى امرأة بالنصف فقامت عليه حتى أدركت فالعليق لصاحب البذر وعلى صاحب البذر قيمة العلف وأجرة مثلها. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث دفع إلى امرأة دود التقوم عليها بنفقتها على أن العليق بينهما نصفان فهو بمنزلة المضارب وكل العليق لصاحب الدود وعليه أجر المثل وثمن الأوراق، ولو غصب من آخر دود القز وبيض الدجاج فأمسكه حتى خرج العليق والفرخ . قال شمس الأئمة الحلواني : إن خرج بنفسه فهو لصاحبه رجل له غريم في مصر آخر فقال لرجل اذهب إليه وطالبه بالدين وإذا قبضت فلك عشرة ففعل فله أجر مثله اهـ. ولقائل أن يقول: هذه مكررة مع قوله فيما سبق وتقبل إن اشترك خياطان أو خياط وصباغ قلنا : ذكر هناك شركة لا الصنائع قصداً وهنا بين ما إذا وقع العقد على شركة الصنائع ضمناً فبهذا الاعتبار لا تكرار .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if a tailor or dyer appoints in his shop someone who throws the cloth to him for half [the profit], it is valid.' This is istihsan, and qiyas is that it is not valid. This issue should really be mentioned in the book of partnership. The face of istihsan is that this is a partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sana'i') and not a hire, because the interpretation of shirkat al-sana'i' is that the work is upon both of them, even if one of them undertakes the work by his skill and the other undertakes acceptance by his prestige. And when it finds a path to validity—and it is customary—it necessitates the opinion of its validity. Thus the work becomes obligatory on both, and the wage is between them according to what is known in its place. The commentator said: The statement of the author of al-Hidayah that this is a partnership of faces (shirkat al-wujuh) contains a type of problem, for shirkat al-wujuh is that they partner to buy with their credit and sell, and in this partnership there is no buying and selling; rather it is a partnership of crafts. He said in al-Ghiyathiyyah: Partnership of acceptance (shirkat al-taqabbul) is to accept work, and here it is not like that; rather they partnered in the resultant of the wage, and it is not a partnership of crafts. And I answered that the partnership in the result necessitates partnership in acceptance, so it is established by implication, for their speech does not specify one as the acceptor and the other as the worker; and specification of something by mention does not negate the ruling from what is other than it, so we established partnership in acceptance by implication. End. And in al-Tatarkhaniyyah: One gave a cow with fodder to another such that the produce is between them half each, so all the increase belongs to the owner of the cow, and he owes the like wage of the recipient and the price of the fodder. Similarly if he gave a chicken to another for half, and if he gave the seed of alfalfa to a woman for half, and she cared for it until it ripened, then the alfalfa belongs to the owner of the seed, and the owner of the seed owes the value of the fodder and her like wage. And in the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: He gave silkworms to a woman to care for them with her expenditure on condition that the silk be between them half each; it is like a mudarib, and all the silk is for the owner of the worms, and he owes the like wage and the price of the leaves. And if one usurped silkworms or chicken eggs from another and kept them until the silk or chick emerged, Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani said: If it emerged by itself, it belongs to its owner. [Regarding] a man who has a debtor in another city, he said to another man: Go to him and demand the debt, and when you collect, you have ten dirhams; he did it, so he has his like wage. End. And one might say: This is repeated with his previous statement: 'If two tailors or a tailor and a dyer partner.' We say: There he mentioned partnership intentionally, not crafts, and here he explains when the contract is on partnership of crafts implicitly; so from this consideration there is no repetition.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (istihsan) regarding a contract where a shopkeeper hires a tailor or dyer to work in his shop, with the condition that the craftsman brings in customers and the shopkeeper provides the premises, sharing the profit equally. The matn (from Kanz al-Daqaiq) declares the contract valid, and the commentator expands on the reasoning. The primary proof is istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas, which would invalidate it due to uncertainty (jahalah) in the wage. The commentator explains that this is not a hire but a partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sana'i'), where both parties contribute—one with skill, the other with prestige—and the profit is shared according to custom. He addresses an objection from al-Hidayah that this resembles shirkat al-wujuh (credit partnership), but clarifies it is a partnership of crafts. He also cites supporting cases from al-Tatarkhaniyyah and Fatawa Abu al-Layth to illustrate the principle in analogous transactions like sharing livestock produce or silk cultivation. Finally, he resolves an apparent repetition by distinguishing between explicit partnership and implicit partnership of crafts.

Key Terms
شركة الصنائع (shirkat al-sana'i'): A partnership where each contributes a craft or skill, sharing the profit from the work.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference to depart from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable ruling, often based on custom (urf) or need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason the Hanafi school validates the contract where a tailor works in a shop for half the profit?
A) It is considered a valid ijarah because the wage is determinable by custom.
B) It is considered a valid partnership of crafts (shirkat al-sana'i') based on istihsan.
C) It is considered a sale of future earnings, which is allowed.
D) It is considered a gift from the shop owner to the tailor.

Key Takeaway
Custom and istihsan validate a shared-profit contract as a partnership of crafts, overriding the strict analogy that would invalidate it due to unknown wage.

Validity of Ijarah with Unspecified Load: Istihsan and 'Urf
Explaining the istihsan ruling for hiring a camel for howdah and two riders with unspecified load

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principles of istihsan and 'urf to determine the validity of an ijarah contract with unspecified load, and distinguish it from cases of gross uncertainty.

Core Idea
An ijarah contract is valid if the unspecified term is resolved by custom — uncertainty is only fatal when it cannot be cured by 'urf.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq demonstrates how the Hanafi school uses istihsan and 'urf to validate contracts that would otherwise be invalid due to uncertainty. The case of hiring a camel to carry a howdah and riders to Mecca without specifying the exact load is contrasted with a previous case of hiring a donkey without specifying anything at all. Understanding this distinction clarifies the boundaries of permissible uncertainty in ijarah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The contract of hiring a camel for a howdah and two riders to Mecca is ruled valid (sahih). Determine what exactly is being ruled upon and the hukm level.
The hukm is sahih, not fasid or batil.
2
Locate the opposing qiyas and proof
Qiyas (analogy) would render it invalid due to jahalah (uncertainty). This is the position of Imam al-Shafi'i. Identify the proof each school uses.
Shafi'i uses qiyas on general principle that uncertainty invalidates.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
The Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) based on 'urf (custom). The uncertainty is removed because custom determines the reasonable load, provisions, etc. for such a journey.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in this case.
4
Distinguish from the previous case
In the earlier case of hiring a donkey without specifying what to carry, no specification was made (gross uncertainty). Here, the type of load (howdah and riders) is specified, only the weight is unspecified. Custom cures this minor uncertainty.
The key difference is specification of the genus vs. complete ambiguity.
5
Note the recommendation for certainty
The commentator recommends that the carrier see the howdah and riders to further reduce uncertainty and ensure consent. This is preferable (ahab) but not required.
This is a matter of caution, not a condition of validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو استأجر حملاً ليحمل عليه محملا وراكبين إلى مكة صح وله المحمل المعتاد والقياس أن لا يجوز للجهالة وهو قول الإمام الشافعي ووجه الاستحسان أن هذه الجهالة تزول بالصرف إلى المتعارف وله المتعارف من الحمل والزاد والغطاء وغير ذلك مما معلوم عند أهل العرف، لا يقال هذه متكررة مع قوله «وإن استأجر حماراً ولم يسم ما يحمل» قلنا : هناك لم يبين ما يحمل فكانت الجهالة فاحشة، وهنا بين ما يحمل الحمل ولم يبين قدره. قال رحمه الله : ( ورؤيته أحب يعني رؤية المكاري المحمل والراكب وما يتبعهما أحب لأنه أبعد من الجهالة أقرب للعمل لتحقق الرضا.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if one hires a camel to carry a howdah and two riders to Mecca, it is valid, and he has the customary howdah. Qiyas is that it is not valid due to uncertainty, and it is the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i. The face of istihsan is that this uncertainty is removed by referring to the customary [standard]; he has the customary load, provision, cover, and other things known among the people of custom. It should not be said that this is repeated with his statement: 'And if he hires a donkey and does not specify what to carry.' We say: There he did not specify what to carry, so the uncertainty was gross; here he specified what to carry (the load) but did not specify its quantity.' He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And seeing it is preferable,' meaning the carrier seeing the howdah, the rider, and their accompaniments is preferable because it distances from uncertainty and brings closer to action for actualizing consent.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (istihsan) regarding the validity of hiring a camel for a specific purpose—carrying a howdah and two riders to Mecca—without specifying the exact weight or details of the load. The matn declares it valid, and the commentator provides the Hanafi reasoning. The proof is istihsan based on custom (urf): since the journey is known and people of custom know the usual load for such a trip, the uncertainty is minor and does not invalidate the contract. This distinguishes it from a previous case where hiring a donkey without specifying anything was gross uncertainty. Additionally, it recommends that the carrier see the howdah and riders to further reduce uncertainty and ensure consent. The opposition (Imam al-Shafi'i) holds it invalid due to uncertainty in the hire.

Key Terms
المتعارف (al-muta'arraf): What is customary or conventionally accepted in a particular society or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the load is negligible.
B) Because custom defines the reasonable load for such a journey.
C) Because the riders are specified, so no uncertainty remains.
D) Because the camel owner has the right to choose the load.

Key Takeaway
Hiring a camel for howdah and riders is valid because custom removes the uncertainty; Hanafi law uses istihsan and 'urf to prevent hardship.

Compensation for Eating Journey Provisions by a Hired Carrier
Ruling on compensation for eating zad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a hired carrier consuming the journey provisions and explain the reasoning behind the obligation to compensate.

Core Idea
A hired carrier must compensate for consuming the entrusted provisions; custom does not override liability.

This lesson examines a specific fiqh issue in the chapter of ijara: whether a hired carrier who eats from the provisions (zad) he is obligated to carry must compensate. The matn states unequivocally that he must. However, some Shafi'i scholars object based on the custom of travelers. We will explore the Hanafi argument and its refutation of the custom-based exception.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The matn rules that the carrier must compensate (رد عوضه). The commentator affirms this as the relied-upon position.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Some Shafi'is argue that custom allows travelers to eat from provisions without compensation, so the absolute contract should be interpreted according to custom.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The carrier is obligated to carry a known quantity; consuming any part breaches that commitment. The analogy of a shepherd drinking milk from sheep under his care shows that trustees must compensate for consumption.
Key proof: qiyas to the shepherd case from al-Khaniyyah.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to custom
The claimed custom is not uniform; some travelers return provisions. Therefore, the general rule of compensation stands, and the action of those who do not return may be due to self-sufficiency, not permissibility.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, compensation is wajib. Under the Shafi'i view, if custom is proven uniform, possibly no compensation. However, the Hanafi school holds that uniformity is not established.
Practical difference: the carrier remains liable in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولمقدار زاد فأكل منه رد عوضه يعني إذا استأجر رجلاً ليحمل عليه مقداراً من الزاد فأكل منه في الطريق رد عوضه وقال بعض الشافعية لا يرد لأن عرف المسافرين أنهم يأكلون الزاد ولا يردون والمطلق يحمل على المتعارف بخلاف الماء حيث يكون له الرد لأن العرف جرى برده . ولنا أنه استحق عليه حمل مقدار معلوم في جميع الطريق قل أو كثر فإذا أكل منه شيئاً فقد استوفى بعض ما التزم حمله فكان عليه رده أو عوضه وتطرق الإنسان إلى أكلهما لا يمنع الضمان كالراعي يرعى الغنم ويأكل من لبنها فإنه يضمن كما في الخانية، وأما دعواهم العرف فمسلم إلا أن العرف غير مطرد فإن بعض المسافرين يردون فلا يلزمنا عرف البعض، أو يحمل فعل من لا يرد على أنهم استغنوا فلا يلزم حجه ويرد بعضهم وهم المحتاجون إليه.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And for a quantity of provision (zad), if he eats from it, he should compensate its substitute,' meaning if one hires a man to carry a quantity of provision, and he eats from it on the way, he must compensate its substitute. Some Shafi'is said he does not compensate because the custom of travelers is that they eat from the provision and do not return it, and the absolute is interpreted according to custom, unlike water where he must return it because custom has established its return. Our argument is that he is obligated to carry a known quantity throughout the entire journey, little or much; so if he eats from it, he has consumed part of what he was committed to carry, so he must return it or its substitute. A person's inclination to eat it does not negate liability, just as a shepherd who grazes sheep and drinks their milk is liable, as in al-Khaniyyah. As for their claim of custom, it is granted, but the custom is not uniform; some travelers return it, so we are not bound by the custom of some. Or the action of those who do not return is interpreted as their being self-sufficient (not needing it), so their argument is not binding; and some do return it—those who are in need of it.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on whether a hired carrier who eats from the journey provisions (zad) must compensate. The matn states he must compensate (رد عوضه). The commentator explains that the carrier is hired to carry a specific quantity; eating any part means he has used what he was entrusted to deliver, so he is liable. He addresses the Shafi'i objection that custom allows travelers to eat without compensation. The Hanafi response is that custom is not consistent; some travelers return the food, so the ruling is based on the principle that the carrier is a trustee (amin) for the delivery, and consuming the subject matter makes him a guarantor. The analogy of a shepherd drinking milk from sheep under his care is given: he must compensate. Thus, the Hanafi position is clear: compensation is required.

Key Terms
رد العوض (radd al-'iwad): Return of the substitute or compensation for the consumed property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a hired carrier eats from the provision he is hired to carry, what is the ruling?
A) He does not need to compensate because it is customary.
B) He must compensate for what he ate.
C) He must compensate only if the amount is significant.
D) He must compensate but only if the owner did not give permission.

Key Takeaway
A hired carrier who consumes the entrusted journey provisions must compensate, as the contract obligates delivery of the full amount, and alleged custom does not override this duty unless proven uniform.

Validity of Adding Contracts to Future Time (Idafa)
Listing contracts valid with idafa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adding contracts to a future time (idafa) and distinguish it from conditional suspension (ta'liq) for ijarah and other contracts.

Core Idea
Idafa (future start) is valid in ijarah and similar contracts; ta'liq (condition) is generally invalid in ijarah.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rule that certain contracts can be validly added to a future time by specifying a later start date. The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq, commented in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, lists which contracts allow such addition and also distinguishes between idafa and ta'liq. You'll learn the base ruling for ijarah and how it extends to other transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for ijarah
The passage states that ijarah is valid when added to a future time (idafa). This is because ijarah is a contract that can take effect moment by moment. For example, adding ijarah to Ramadan while in Sha'ban is valid.
Idafa means specifying a future start date, not suspending the contract on a condition.
2
Distinguish idafa from ta'liq
The passage contrasts idafa with ta'liq (suspension). Saying 'I lease you this house tomorrow' is idafa (valid). Saying 'When tomorrow comes, I have leased you this house' is ta'liq (invalid for ijarah).
Ta'liq introduces a condition; ijarah dislikes such uncertainty (gharar).
3
Trace the extension to other contracts
The ruling extends to muzara'ah (sharecropping), mu'amalah, musaqah (orchard tenure), mudarabah (profit-sharing), wakalah (agency), and kafalah (suretyship). Each is explained with nuances.
Kafalah allows suspension only with a customary condition, not an absolute one.
4
Note inherent additions
Isa' (executorship) and wasiyyah (bequest) are inherently added to after death. Qada (judgeship) and imarah (governorship) can be suspended, as shown by the Prophet's example.
These are delegations, so suspension is allowed.
5
Recognize the scope for talaq, 'itq, and waqf
Divorce, manumission, and waqf are also valid when added to a future time (mudafan). The passage ends by affirming this.
The passage cuts off but the ruling is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وتصح الإجارة وفسخها لأن الإجارة تنعقد ساعة فساعة وهذا معنى الإضافة وفسخها يعتبر بها كما إذا أضاف الإجارة إلى رمضان وهو في شعبان وكذا إذا أضاف الفسخ إلى شوال وهو في رمضان. وفي القنية : إذا قال أجرتك هذه الدار غداً يجوز، ولو قال إذا جاء غد قد أجرتك هذا الدار باطل لأنه تعليق. وقال أبو بكر : تجوز في اللفظين ولا خطر في في ا وبه يفتي. وعن ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف أجرتك داري بكذا إذا هل كذا يجوز في الإجارة ولا يجوز في البيع. قال رحمه الله: (والزارعة والمعاملة يعني وتصح المزارعة أيضاً بالإضافة إلى المستقبل كما إذا قال وهو في شعبان زارعتك أرضي من أول رمضان بكذا، وتصح أيضاً المساقاة بأن قال ساقيتك بستاني من أول رمضان وهو شعبان بكذا لأن المزارعة وهي والمعاملة إجارة فتعتبر بالإجارة . قال رحمه الله : (والمضاربة والوكالة) لأنهما من باب الإطلاق وكل ذلك تجوز إضافته. قال رحمه الله : (والكفالة) لأنها التزام للمال ابتداء فتجوز إضافتها وتعليقها بالشرط كالبذر لكن فيها تمليك المطالبة فلا يجوز تعليقها بالشرط المطلق بل بالشرط المتعارف . قال رحمه الله : (والإيصاء والوصية والإيصاء إقامة الشخص مقام نفسه، والوصية التمليك وكلامهما مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت لأنهما لا يكونان إلا مضافين إذ الإيصاء في الحال لا يتصور إلا إذا جعل مجازاً عن الوكالة . قال رحمه الله : ( والقضاء والإمارة) يجوز تعليقهما بالشرط وإضافتهما إلى الزمان لأنهما تولية وتفويض محض فجاز تعليقهما بالشرط، والأصل في ذلك أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر زيد بن حارثة ثم قال : إن قتل زيد فجعفر وإن قتل جعفر فعبد الله بن رواحة. قال رحمه الله : والطلاق والعتق والوقف مضافاً) لا يخفى أن قوله «مضافاً» نصب على الحال وهو قيد للمذكورات كلها وتقدير الكلام ويصح كل
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And ijarah and its rescission are valid because ijarah is valid moment by moment, and this is the meaning of addition (idafa). Its rescission is considered like it, as when he adds the ijarah to Ramadan while he is in Sha'ban, and similarly if he adds the rescission to Shawwal while in Ramadan. In al-Qunyah: If he says "I lease this house to you tomorrow," it is valid. If he says "When tomorrow comes, I have leased this house to you," it is invalid because it is a suspension (ta'liq). Abu Bakr said: It is valid in both wordings, and there is no harm in it, and by it fatwa is given. From Ibn Sama'ah from Abu Yusuf: 'I lease my house to you at such-and-such when such-and-such is due' is valid in ijarah but not in sale.' He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And muzara'ah and mu'amalah,' i.e., muzara'ah is also valid when added to the future, like when he says in Sha'ban 'I make muzara'ah with you on my land starting from Ramadan for so-and-so.' And also musaqah is valid by saying 'I contract musaqah with you on my garden starting from Ramadan' while he is in Sha'ban, because muzara'ah and mu'amalah are ijarah, so they follow ijarah. He said: 'And mudarabah and wakalah' because they are from the category of absolute permission, and all of these allow addition. He said: 'And kafalah' because it is an initial commitment of wealth, so its addition and suspension by a condition are allowed like the seed, but it involves giving the right to claim, so its suspension by an absolute condition is not allowed; rather by a customary condition. He said: 'And isa' (executorship) and wasiyyah (bequest); isa' is placing a person in one's own position, and wasiyyah is transfer of ownership. Their statement is added to after death because they only occur as additions, since isa' in the present is inconceivable unless it is used metaphorically for wakalah.' He said: 'And judiciary (qada) and emirate (imarah)' – it is permissible to suspend them by condition and add them to time because they are pure delegation and empowerment, so suspension by condition is allowed. The basis for that is that the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded Zaid ibn Harithah, then said: 'If Zaid is killed, then Ja'far; if Ja'far is killed, then 'Abdullah ibn Rawahah.' He said: 'And divorce (talaq), manumission ('itq), and waqf, when added (mudafan).' It is not hidden that his word 'mudafan' is in the accusative as a circumstantial qualifier, and it is a restriction for all the mentioned items. The appreciation of the statement is: 'And every'
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings on which contracts are valid when added to a future time (idafa) or suspended by a condition. The base ruling is that ijarah (hire) can be added to a future time because it is a continuous contract that can take effect later. Rescission (faskh) can also be added by analogy. The commentator distinguishes between idafa (specifying a future start time) and ta'liq (suspension with a condition like 'if tomorrow comes'); the latter is invalid for ijarah according to the matn, though Abu Bakr permitted both. Then it extends the same principle to muzara'ah (sharecropping), mu'amalah (a type of partnership), musaqah (orchard sharecropping), mudarabah (profit-sharing), wakalah (agency), and kafalah (suretyship), each with specific notes. Kafalah allows suspension but only with a customary condition. Isa' (executorship) and wasiyyah (will) are inherently added to after death. Qada (judgeship) and imarah (governorship) can be suspended, as shown by the Prophet's instruction. Finally, talaq, 'itq, and waqf are valid when added to a future time. The passage provides a comprehensive view of the Hanafi ruling on temporal addition in contracts.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idafa): Adding a contract to a future time by specifying a later start date, not by suspending it with a condition.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the contract's validity upon the occurrence of a future condition, which is generally invalid in ijarah but valid in some other contracts like wakalah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is invalid in Hanafi fiqh?
A) Adding ijarah to Ramadan from Sha'ban.
B) Adding rescission of ijarah to Shawwal from Ramadan.
C) Saying 'If tomorrow comes, I have leased this house to you.'
D) Saying 'I lease this house to you tomorrow.'

Key Takeaway
Idafa (specifying a future start) is generally valid in ijarah and many contracts, but ta'liq (conditional suspension) is invalid in ijarah except where expressly allowed.

Rulings on Hiring for Carrying: Customary Load and Eating from Food
Commentary on rulings about hired carrier's food consumption and visualization of load

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a hired carrier eating from the carried food, explain the role of custom in the dispute with Shafi'is, and apply the qiyas with water.

Core Idea
A hired carrier who eats from the food must compensate because the obligation to deliver the full amount remains.

This lesson examines two rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: the recommendation to visualize the load to avoid uncertainty, and the obligation of a hired carrier to compensate if he eats from the food he transports. The focus is on the second ruling, where the Hanafi school engages in a sharp debate with some Shafi'is over the force of custom. You will see how qiyas (analogy) with water and a critique of custom's uniformity underpin the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, the matn says seeing the load is preferable (aḥab). Second, if the carrier eats from the food, he must return its value (radda 'iwadah). The commentary expands each.
The first ruling is about reducing uncertainty (jahāla), the second about contractual obligation.
2
Analyze the first ruling on visualization
The commentator explains that 'seeing' refers to the hirer viewing the carrier, load, and rider to ensure consent and minimize uncertainty. This is recommended (aḥab) but not obligatory.
Uncertainty (jahāla) in the contract is minor here because the load is specified but quantity may be uncertain; seeing it helps.
3
Examine the dispute on eating from food
The Hanafi position: the carrier must compensate. Some Shafi'is say no, because custom ('urf) of travelers is to eat without repayment. The Hanafi response uses qiyas with water and critiques custom.
The issue is whether custom overrides the explicit contractual duty.
4
Trace the Hanafi dalil and qiyas
The carrier is obligated (mustahiq) to carry a known amount for the entire journey; eating prevents fulfillment. This is analogous to water, where compensation is agreed. The 'illa is both are consumables whose consumption hinders full delivery.
The qiyas jaliyy (clear analogy) with water is used; objections about custom are answered.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis require compensation; Shafi'is do not, relying on custom. The Hanafi tarjih (preference) is that custom is not uniform, so the analogy stands.
Understanding this dispute clarifies how custom is weighed against contractual terms in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعتاد ورؤيته أحب والمقدار زاد فأكل منه رد عوضه
بين الأجارة فكانت يسيرة لأنه قلنا : هناك لم يبين ما يحمل فكانت الجهالة فاحشة، وهنا بين ما يحمل الحمل ولم يبين قدره
قال رحمه الله : ورؤيته أحب يعني رؤية المكاري المحمل والراكب وما يتبعهما أحب لأنه أبعد من الجهالة أقرب للعمل لتحقق الرضا.
قال رحمه الله : (ولمقدار زاد فأكل منه رد عوضه يعني إذا استأجر رجلاً ليحمل عليه مقداراً من الزاد فأكل منه في الطريق رد عوضه
وقال بعض الشافعية لا يرد لأن عرف المسافرين أنهم يأكلون الزاد ولا يردون والمطلق يحمل على المتعارف بخلاف الماء حيث يكون له الرد لأن العرف جرى برده .
ولنا أنه استحق عليه حمل مقدار معلوم في جميع الطريق قله أن يستوفيه فصار كالماء والعرف مشترك فإن بعض المسافرين يردون فلا يلزمنا عرف البعض، أو يحمل فعل من لا يرد على أنهم استغنوا فلا يلزم حجه ويرد بعضهم وهم المحتاجون إليه.
The customary (load) and seeing it is preferable. If the amount (of food) increases, and he eats from it, he must compensate its value. Explanation of the hire: it was minor because we said: there, he did not specify what to carry, so the uncertainty was severe. Here, he specified the load but not its quantity. He (the author) said: seeing it is preferable, meaning seeing the camel driver, the load, and the rider and what accompanies them is preferable because it is further from uncertainty and closer to action to realize consent. He (the author) said: for the amount of food, if he eats from it, he must compensate its value, meaning if he hires a man to carry an amount of food and he eats from it on the way, he must compensate its value. Some Shafi'is said he does not compensate because the custom of travelers is that they eat the food and do not compensate, and the absolute is carried on the customary, unlike water where he has to compensate because custom has established compensation. Our proof: he is obligated to carry a known amount for the entire journey—he cannot fulfill it—so it becomes like water. Custom is shared: some travelers compensate, so the custom of some is not binding on us. Or the action of those who do not compensate is interpreted as they were self-sufficient, so his argument is not binding, and some compensate, namely those who need it.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on two rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding contracts of hire (ijāra) for carrying goods. First, the matn states that seeing the load (the customary amount) is preferable (aḥab). The commentator explains that 'customary' here refers to what is usual, and 'seeing' means the hirer seeing the camel, load, and rider to reduce uncertainty (jahāla) and ensure mutual consent. This is not obligatory but recommended. Second, the matn states that if the hired carrier eats from the food he is carrying, he must compensate. The commentator expands: if a man hires another to carry a specified amount of food, and the carrier eats some during the journey, he must pay back its value. Some Shāfi'īs argue he does not compensate because the custom of travelers is to eat freely without repayment, and contracts are interpreted according to custom. However, the Hanafī response is that the carrier is obligated to carry a fixed amount the entire journey—he cannot fulfill that if he eats any part—so it is analogous to water, where it is agreed he must compensate. Custom is not uniform: some travelers do repay, and those who do not may have been self-sufficient, so custom does not excuse the obligation. The Hanafī ruling (Abū Hanīfa) is that compensation is required.

Key Terms
المكاري (al-mukārī): The person who hires out a camel or mount for transport; in this context, the carrier.
الزاد (al-zād): Food provisions carried by a traveler.
الجهالة (al-jahālah): Uncertainty or ambiguity in a contract that may render it invalid if severe (fāḥish).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafī position in this passage, what is the ruling if a hired carrier eats from the food he is carrying?
A) He does not compensate because of custom
B) He must compensate because the obligation to carry the full amount remains
C) He must compensate only if the food is water
D) The contract becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
A hired carrier must compensate for food he eats because the contractual duty to deliver the full amount is not overridden by a non-uniform custom.

When Can You Add a Contract to a Future Time?
Listing valid and invalid contracts for iḍāfa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which contracts are valid when added to a future time (iḍāfa) and which are not, cite the rationale (tamlik vs. non-tamlik), and apply the rule to new cases.

Core Idea
Contracts that transfer immediate ownership cannot be added to the future—only those that are renewed moment by moment or are not pure tamlik.

The Hanafi school allows you to start many contracts at a future date (iḍāfa), like renting a house from next month or appointing an agent from tomorrow. But not all contracts can be pushed forward. Why? The key is whether the contract involves an immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik). In this lesson, we explore the passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq with its commentary, learning which contracts are valid with iḍāfa and which are not, and the reasoning behind the exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Separate valid and invalid contracts
Read the list of contracts mentioned: ijarah, muzara'ah, musaqah, muḍarabah, wakalah, kafalah, īṣā', waṣiyyah, qaḍā', imārah, ṭalāq, 'itq, waqf are valid. Sale, its ratification/annulment, division, partnership, gift, marriage, revocation, settlement, discharge of debt are invalid. Write them in two columns.
The invalid list starts with 'لا البيع' (not sale).
2
Identify the principle
Notice the reasoning in the text: the invalid contracts are described as 'tamlik' (ownership transfer) that can be executed immediately, so there is no need for iḍāfa. Valid contracts either are not tamlik (e.g., wakālah, kafālah) or renew moment by moment (e.g., ijārah). Internalise: 'tamlik' is the blocker.
Tamlik includes not just transfer of property but also rights like marriage (which is tamlik of the woman's right).
3
Note the exception to the exception
Even among valid contracts, there is a nuance: pure addition (iḍāfa) is valid, but suspension (ta'liq) on a condition is generally invalid. However, kafālah can be suspended on a customary condition. Also, for ijārah, different wordings ('tomorrow' vs 'when tomorrow comes') were debated.
The text mentions Abu Bakr's view allowing both wordings, and that this is the mufta bihi.
4
Apply to a new contract
Consider a modern case: 'I will license my software to you from January 1st.' Licencing is like ijārah (permission to use). Since it is not a sale of ownership, it should be valid with iḍāfa. Contrast: 'I sell you this car from January 1st'—invalid because sale is tamlik.
This step uses the principle to extend to new situations.
5
Memorise the two lists
For fatwa and exam, you need to recall which contracts go where. Use a mnemonic: contracts that are temporary or authorizations are valid; contracts that transfer full ownership are invalid.
Review the list in the passage several times.

Source Text

قال رحمه الله : (وتصح الإجارة وفسخها لأن الإجارة تنعقد ساعة فساعة وهذا معنى الإضافة وفسخها يعتبر بها كما إذا أضاف الإجارة إلى رمضان وهو في شعبان وكذا إذا أضاف الفسخ إلى شوال وهو في رمضان. وفي القنية : إذا قال أجرتك هذه الدار غداً يجوز، ولو قال إذا جاء غد قد أجرتك هذا هذه الدار باطل لأنه تعليق. وقال أبو بكر : تجوز في اللفظين ولا خطر في في [؟] وبه يفتي. وعن ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف أجرتك داري بكذا إذا هل كذا يجوز في الإجارة ولا يجوز في البيع. قال رحمه الله: (والزارعة والمعاملة يعني وتصح المزارعة أيضاً بالإضافة إلى المستقبل كما إذا قال وهو في شعبان زارعتك أرضي من أول رمضان بكذا، وتصح أيضاً المساقاة بأن قال ساقيتك بستاني من أول رمضان وهو شعبان بكذا لأن المزارعة وهي والمعاملة إجارة فتعتبر بالإجارة . قال رحمه الله : (والمضاربة والوكالة) لأنهما من باب الإطلاق وكل ذلك تجوز إضافته. قال رحمه الله : (والكفالة) لأنها التزام للمال ابتداء فتجوز إضافتها وتعليقها بالشرط كالبذر لكن فيها تمليك المطالبة فلا يجوز تعليقها بالشرط المطلق بل بالشرط المتعارف . قال رحمه الله : (والإيصاء والوصية والإيصاء إقامة الشخص مقام نفسه، والوصية التمليك وكلامهما مضاف إلى ما بعد الموت لأنهما لا يكونان إلا مضافين إذ الإيصاء في الحال لا يتصور إلا إذا جعل مجازاً عن الوكالة . قال رحمه الله : ( والقضاء والإمارة) يجوز تعليقهما بالشرط وإضافتهما إلى الزمان لأنهما تولية وتفويض محض فجاز تعليقهما بالشرط، والأصل في ذلك أنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أمر زيد بن حارثة ثم قال : إن قتل زيد فجعفر وإن قتل جعفر فعبد الله بن رواحة. قال رحمه الله : والطلاق والعتق والوقف مضافاً) لا يخفى أن قوله «مضافاً» نصب على الحال وهو قيد للمذكورات كلها وتقدير الكلام ويصح كل واحد منها حال كونه مضافاً إلى الزمان المستقبل . قال رحمه الله : (لا البيع وإجازته وفسخه والقسمة والشركة والهبة والنكاح والرجعة والصلح عن مال وإبراء الدين) يعني هذه الأشياء لا يجوز إضافتها إلى الزمان المستقبل لأنها تمليك وقد أمكن تنجيزها للحال فلا حاجة إلى الإضافة وقد تقدم والله تعالى أعلم.
He (the author) said: Ijarah and its annulment are valid (when added to future time), because ijarah is contracted hour by hour, and this is the meaning of addition (iḍāfa), and its annulment is considered similarly. For example, if he adds ijarah to Ramadan while in Sha'ban, and similarly if he adds annulment to Shawwal while in Ramadan. In al-Qunyah: If he says 'I rent you this house tomorrow' it is valid. If he says 'When tomorrow comes, I have rented you this house' it is invalid because it is a suspension (ta'liq). Abu Bakr said: It is permissible in both expressions, and there is no harm... and by it a fatwa is given. From Ibn Sama'a from Abu Yusuf: 'I rented you my house for such-and-such when such-and-such occurs' is valid in ijarah but not in sale. He said: Muzara'ah and mu'amalah, meaning muzara'ah is also valid when added to the future, as if he says while in Sha'ban: I contract muzara'ah with you on my land from the beginning of Ramadan for such-and-such. Musaqah is also valid if he says: I contract musaqah with you on my garden from the beginning of Ramadan while in Sha'ban, because muzara'ah and mu'amalah are ijarah, so they are treated like ijarah. He said: Mudarabah and wakalah, because they are from the category of absolute authorization, and all of that is permissible to add. He said: Kafalah, because it is an undertaking of wealth from the beginning, so it is permissible to add it and suspend it on a condition like seeds, but it includes ownership of the right to demand, so it is not permissible to suspend it on an absolute condition, but rather on a customary condition. He said: Iṣa' and waṣiyyah; iṣa' is appointing someone in one's place, and waṣiyyah is ownership, and both are added to after death because they are only added, as iṣa' in the present is not conceivable unless it is metaphorical for wakalah. He said: Qaḍa' and imarah, it is permissible to suspend them on a condition and add them to time, because they are pure delegation and assignment, so it is permissible to suspend them on a condition. The basis for this is that he (peace be upon him) appointed Zaid bin Haritha, then said: 'If Zaid is killed, then Ja'far; if Ja'far is killed, then Abdullah bin Rawahah.' He said: Talaq, itq, and waqf, when added. It is not hidden that his saying 'added' is in the accusative as a circumstantial qualifier, a restriction for all the mentioned ones, and the meaning of the speech is: each one of them is valid when added to a future time. He said: Not sale, its ratification and annulment, dividing (qismah), partnership (sharikah), gift (hibah), marriage (nikah), revocation (raj'ah), settlement for property (ṣulḥu 'an māl), and discharge of debt (ibrā' al-dayn). Meaning these things are not permissible to add to a future time because they are ownership (tamlik) and immediate execution is possible, so there is no need for addition, and it has preceded. And Allah Almighty knows best.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a listing of contracts and legal acts that are valid or invalid when 'added' to a future time (iḍāfa ilā al-zamān al-mustaqbal). The commentator from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. Ijāra (hire) and its annulment are valid with addition because the contract is renewed hour by hour; addition means the effect begins at a future time. Suspension (ta'liq) with a condition is invalid, but pure addition (adding to a time without condition) is valid. The same applies to muzāra'ah (crop-sharing), musāqāh (irrigation contract), muḍārabah (partnership), wakālah (agency), kafālah (surety), īṣā' (appointment of executor), waṣiyyah (bequest), qaḍā' (judgeship), imārah (leadership), ṭalāq (divorce), 'itq (emancipation), and waqf (endowment). These are valid when added to a future time. However, sale (bay'), its ratification and annulment, division (qismah), partnership (sharikah), gift (hibah), marriage (nikāḥ), revocation (raj'ah), settlement (ṣulḥ), and discharge of debt (ibrā') are not valid with addition because they are contracts of ownership (tamlik), and immediate execution is possible, so no benefit in addition. The passage also includes a specific ruling from al-Qunyah and Abu Yusuf's opinion on different wordings for ijarah addition.

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-iḍāfah): Adding the effect of a contract to a future time without a condition (e.g., 'rent you from Ramadan' while in Sha'ban).
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspending the contract on a future event (e.g., 'if tomorrow comes, I rent you'). Generally invalid except where stated (like kafalah with customary condition).
التمليك (al-tamlik): Ownership transfer; contracts that effect immediate transfer of ownership cannot be added to future time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT valid when added to a future time according to this passage?
A) Muzāra'ah (crop-sharing)
B) Wakālah (agency)
C) Ṣulḥu 'an māl (settlement for property)
D) Kafālah (surety)

Key Takeaway
Addition to a future time (iḍāfa) is valid for contracts that are not immediate ownership transfers; sale, marriage, and similar tamlik contracts cannot be added.
