Ruling on Adding Certain Contracts to Future Time
States the ruling that certain contracts cannot be made contingent on a future time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that tamlik contracts cannot be added to future time and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Contracts transferring ownership must be immediate; future contingencies invalidate them.

In Hanafi fiqh, contracts that transfer ownership (tamlik) must be executed on the spot. You cannot say 'I sell you this next month' or 'I gift you this tomorrow.' This lesson examines the list of such contracts and the reasoning behind the prohibition, drawing a clear line with contracts like ijarah that naturally involve future usufruct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of contracts
Read the passage and note all contracts mentioned: sale, ratification, rescission, division, partnership, gift, marriage, return, settlement for property, and release of debt.
Memorize these ten as they are the core of this ruling.
2
Understand the common element: tamlik
Each contract involves an immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik). For example, sale transfers ownership of the item; gift transfers ownership without compensation.
This is the key reason for the ruling.
3
Apply the principle
If a contract is tamlik and can be executed now, there is no need to add it to a future time. Doing so renders the contract invalid.
The exception is ijarah, which involves usufruct over time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (لا البيع وإجازته وفسخه والقسمة والشركة والهبة والنكاح والرجعة والصلح عن مال وإبراء الدين) يعني هذه الأشياء لا يجوز إضافتها إلى الزمان المستقبل لأنها تمليك وقد أمكن تنجيزها للحال فلا حاجة إلى الإضافة وقد تقدم والله تعالى أعلم.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Not the sale and its ratification and its rescission, and the division and partnership and gift and marriage and return and settlement for property and release of debt) meaning these things are not permissible to be added to the future time because they are a transfer of ownership and it is possible to execute them immediately, so there is no need for addition, and it has preceded, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq issues a clear ruling: certain contracts cannot be concluded as contingent on a future time. These include sale, its ratification, its rescission, division (qisma), partnership (sharika), gift (hiba), marriage (nikah), return (ruj'a), settlement (sulh) for property, and release of debt (ibra'). The reason is that all these acts involve immediate transfer of ownership (tamlik). Since they can be performed on the spot, there is no legitimate need to postpone them, unlike contracts such as ijarah (hire) which inherently relate to future benefits. The author concludes by noting that this ruling has been discussed earlier (in the book of ijarah).

Key Terms
إضافة إلى الزمان المستقبل (idafah ila al-zaman al-mustaqbal): Attaching a contract's effect to a future time, e.g., saying 'I sell you this next month.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Tamlik contracts must be executed immediately; adding them to a future time renders them invalid.

Definition of Ma'dhun: Lifting Interdiction and Dropping Right
Defines the authorized slave with talil reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ma'dhun and explain why it is considered a dropping of the master's right (isqat al-haqq) rather than a delegation of authority.

Core Idea
Ma'dhun is defined as lifting interdiction and dropping the master's right — the slave transacts with his original capacity, not as an agent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines the foundational concept of the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun) in Hanafi fiqh. The definition centers on two elements: lifting the legal interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and dropping the master's right (isqat al-haqq). The author then provides a talil (reasoning) to justify why the slave's transactions become valid upon permission, and why this permission is not a form of agency but a complete relinquishment of the master's protective right. Understanding this definition is crucial for the rules governing the ma'dhun's capacity to trade and incur liabilities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-part definition of ma'dhun
Locate the phrases 'fakk al-hajr' (lifting of interdiction) and 'isqat al-haqq' (dropping of the right) in the passage. These are the key components that define the authorized slave in the Hanafi school.
This definition distinguishes the Hanafi view from other schools that treat permission as agency.
2
Understand the rationale behind the definition
The passage explains that the slave inherently possesses the pillars of disposition (articulate speech and a capacity suitable for undertaking rights), but was interdicted to protect the master's property. When the master grants permission, he drops his right, restoring the slave's original eligibility.
The reasoning is talil: the slave's original capacity is only blocked, not absent.
3
Distinguish isqat al-haqq from wikalah
Note that the passage concludes that because the permission is a dropping of right, the slave acts with his own eligibility and does not have recourse against the master for liabilities. This contrasts with agency, where the agent acts on behalf of the principal and may seek indemnity.
This distinction has practical implications: the master cannot restrict the permission by time, place, or type.
4
Identify the consequence: no recourse
The text states that the slave does not have recourse against the master for debts incurred. This follows from the nature of isqat: the master has abandoned his right to object, so he is not liable for the slave's transactions.
This rule prevents the master from being held responsible for the slave's trading losses.
5
Note the inclusivity: absolute lifting
The passage says 'he made it absolute in lifting interdiction so it includes complete and partial'. This means the definition covers both full authorization (complete) and partial authorization (e.g., with a partner). The permission is not restricted by type or time.
This is consistent with the view that isqat al-haqq cannot be limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو كتاب المأذون فك الحجر وإسقاط الحق لأن العبد أهل للتصرف بعد الرق لأن ركن التصرف كلام معتبر شرعاً من مميز ومحل التصرف ذمة صالحة لالتزام الحقوق وهما لا يقومان بالرق لأنهما من كرامات البشر إلا أنه حجر عليه عن التصرف لحق المولى لئلا يبطل حقه بتعلق الدين برقبته لضعف ذمة الرقيق، فإذا أذن له المولى فقد أسقط حقه فكان متصرفاً بأهليته الأصلية ولهذا لا يرجع على المولى بما لحقه من العهدة. أطلق في فك الحجر فشمل الكل والبعض.
It is the book of the authorized slave: lifting of interdiction and dropping of the right, because the slave is capable of disposition after slavery because the pillar of disposition is speech considered legally from a discerning person, and the locus of disposition is a legal capacity suitable for undertaking rights; both exist in the slave because they are from the honors of humanity, except that he was interdicted from disposing for the right of the master lest his right be invalidated by the attachment of debt to his person due to the weakness of the slave's capacity. So when the master gave him permission, he has dropped his right, so he acts with his original eligibility, and for this reason he does not have recourse against the master for the liability that befalls him. He made it absolute in lifting interdiction, so it includes complete and partial.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It begins by defining the ma'dhun (authorized slave) in the Hanafi school. The definition has two parts: 'fakk al-hajr' (lifting of interdiction) and 'isqat al-haqq' (dropping of the right). The explanation clarifies that the slave inherently has the capacity to transact (since the pillar of a contract is valid speech from a discerning person, and the locus is a capacity (dhimmah) fit for obligations). However, the master's right prevents the slave from transacting to protect the master's property from being seized for debts. When the master grants permission, he drops his own right, and the slave then acts with his original eligibility. Consequently, the slave does not claim indemnity from the master for debts incurred. The phrase 'he made it absolute in lifting interdiction so it includes complete and partial' means the definition covers full authorization and partial (e.g., with one partner). This establishes that authorization is an act of dropping a right, not a delegation of authority, which has implications for whether it can be restricted.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): A slave who is given permission by his master to engage in trade; that permission lifts the master's right of interdiction.
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Lifting the legal interdiction that prevented the slave from transacting.
إسقاط الحق (isqat al-haqq): Dropping the master's right that prevented the slave's disposition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of the master's permission (idhn) to his slave?
A) It is a form of agency (wikalah).
B) It is a dropping of the master's right (isqat al-haqq).
C) It is a loan of the slave's services.
D) It is a temporary suspension of the master's right.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of ma'dhun as isqat al-haqq (dropping of right) means the slave transacts with original eligibility, not as an agent; thus the master's right is fully relinquished and cannot be restricted.

Authorization by One Partner: Comprehensive Rulings from al-Mabsut
Rulings on authorization by one partner in a slave: debts, earnings, disputes, liability, indivisibility, and ownership changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi rulings when one partner authorizes a slave for trade, including validity, financial allocation, dispute resolution, liability for destruction, and the effects of ownership changes.

Core Idea
One partner's authorization binds only his share; debts follow his share, but earnings are split equally.

This lesson from al-Mabsut presents a series of practical rulings on partnership and authorization. When one co-owner authorizes a slave for trade, his action is valid only for his share, and the silent partner cannot revoke it. The text then addresses financial liability, disputes over earnings, destruction of property, the indivisibility of authorization, and special cases like buying the other partner's share. Understanding these rulings is essential for grasping how Hanafi fiqh handles mixed ownership and agency in commercial contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule: one partner's authorization
Recognize that authorization by one partner is valid only for his own share; the other partner cannot nullify it. This is the foundational principle.
2
Trace the financial effects: debts and earnings
Debt from trade falls on the authorizing partner's share. Earnings from trade (and gifts/charity) after authorization are split equally between both partners. In disputes, the word of the authorizing partner and slave is taken by istihsan.
Earnings before authorization are also split equally.
3
Determine liability for destruction: proof vs confession
If the slave destroys property and it is proven by evidence or eyewitness, both partners are liable. If the slave confesses, only the authorizing partner is liable.
4
Apply the indivisibility principle
Authorization cannot be partial: authorizing half the slave makes the whole slave authorized. Similarly, if one partner asks the other to authorize his share and he does, the slave is fully authorized.
5
Analyze ownership changes and limits
If the authorizing partner later buys the other's share, the slave's knowledge matters: if unaware, debt is on first half; if aware, on entire person. Authorizing a slave not yet owned is invalid even after acquiring ownership. Informing the market of disapproval does not invalidate the authorization by istihsan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في المبسوط : وإذا أذن أحد الشريكين لعبده في التجارة جاز في نصيبه خاصة، وليس للشريك الآخر أن يبطل الإذن وما لحقه من دين التجارة فهو على نصيبه خاصة ولو لحقه دين التجارة وفي يده مال التجارة قضى من دينه والباقي بينهما نصفين لأنه حصل من كسب العبد ولو وهب له أو اكتسب قبل الإذن أو تصدق عليه أو بعد الإذن فهو بينهما نصفين ولو اختلفا في الكسب الذي في يده فقال الآذن والعبد إنه استفاده بالتجارة وقال الساكت إنه استفاده بالهبة فالقول قول الآذن والعبد ويصرفه في دينه استحساناً لأن العبد هو الكاسب وهو أعلم بحال كسبه. ولو استهلك مالاً كان عليهما إذا ثبت بالبينة أو بالمعاينة ويتعلق بجميع رقبته ولو أقر باستهلاك ما كان على الآذن خاصة. ولو أذن رجل بنصف عبده كان مأذوناً في كله لأن الإذن لا يتجزئ، ولو أذن أحد الشريكين ثم اشترى نصيب الآخر فتصرف وهو لا يعلم فالدين كله في النصف الأول، ولو علم بتصرفه ففي جميع الرقبة ولو أذن لعبد لا يملكه ثم ملكه فإنه لا يصير مأذوناً، ولو أخبر شريكه أهل السوق أنه لا يرضى بإذن شريكه ثم رأى العبد يتصرف لم يصر استحساناً. قال أحدهما لشريكه ائذن لنصيبك فأذن له فهو مأذون كله لأن الإذن مما لا يتجزئ فصار كأنه قال لصاحبه ائذن لجميع العبد.
And it is said in al-Mabsut: When one of two partners authorizes his slave for trade, it is valid with respect to his share specifically, and the other partner does not have the right to nullify the authorization. Any debt incurred from trade is on his share specifically. And if a trade debt occurs and he has trade goods in his hand, he pays from his debt and the remainder is between them in halves because it came from the slave's earnings. And if something is gifted to him or he earned before the authorization, or charity given to him, or after authorization, it is between them in halves. And if they dispute the earnings in his hand, the authorizing partner and the slave saying it was from trade and the silent partner saying it was from gift, the statement is of the authorizing partner and the slave, and he spends it on his debt, by istihsan, because the slave is the earner and knows best his earning. And if he destroys property, it is upon both if established by evidence or eyewitness, and it attaches to all of his person. And if he confesses to destruction, it is upon the authorizing partner only. And if a man authorizes half of his slave, he is authorized completely because authorization is indivisible. And if one of the partners authorized, then bought the other's share, and the slave transacts while not knowing, the entire debt is on the first half. And if he knew of his transaction, it is on the entire person. And if he authorized a slave he does not own, then later owns him, he does not become authorized. And if one partner informed the people of the market that he does not consent to his partner's authorization, then saw the slave transacting, it does not become, by istihsan. One said to his partner: 'Authorize your share,' so he authorized him, then he is fully authorized because authorization is indivisible, and it becomes as if he said to his companion: 'Authorize all of the slave.'
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents a series of rulings (masa'il) on partnership and authorization, drawn from al-Mabsut. The first ruling: if one of two co-owners authorizes the slave for trade, it is valid only for that partner's share; the silent partner cannot cancel it. Debts incurred are on that partner's share. If the slave has trade goods, debts are paid from that, and the remainder is split equally because it is from the slave's earnings (which belong to the partners proportionally). Gifts to the slave, earnings before authorization, and charity are also split equally between the partners. If there is a dispute about the nature of earnings (trade vs gift), the word of the authorizing partner and the slave is taken because the slave knows his own earnings (istihsan). If the slave destroys property, liability is on both partners if proven; if he confesses, only on the authorizing partner. If a man authorizes half of his slave (to a third party?), the slave becomes fully authorized because authorization is indivisible. If one partner authorizes, then later buys the other's share, and the slave transacts without knowing, debt is on the first half only; if the slave knew, on the entire person (since the slave is aware of the change in ownership). Authorizing a slave one does not own is invalid even if later acquired. Informing the market of disapproval does not invalidate the authorization by istihsan. Finally, if one partner asks the other to authorize his share and he does, the slave is fully authorized because authorization cannot be partial.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission given by a master or partner to a slave to engage in trade.
كسب العبد (kasb al-'abd): Earnings of the slave; in partnership, they belong to the owners in proportion to their shares.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; here applied to accept the slave's statement about earnings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If one of two co-owners of a slave authorizes the slave for trade, and the slave incurs a debt, who bears the debt?
A) Both co-owners equally.
B) Only the co-owner who authorized.
C) The slave himself.
D) The co-owner who did not authorize.

Key Takeaway
When one partner authorizes a jointly owned slave, his authorization is valid only for his share, but the other partner cannot revoke it, and any debt from trade falls on the authorizer alone.

Authorization Cannot Be Restricted: Hanafi vs Shafi'i and Zufar
Inter-madhhab dispute on whether authorization can be restricted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that authorization is unrestricted, explain the opposing view of Shafi'i and Zufar, and apply the principle of isqat to determine the validity of restrictions in authorization.

Core Idea
Authorization (idhn) is a dropping of right, not delegation; therefore it cannot be restricted by time, place, or type.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a foundational dispute in Hanafi fiqh: can a master restrict his slave's trading authority? The Hanafi school holds that once the master permits his slave to trade, the permission is absolute—any attempt to limit it is void. This is because the permission effectively drops the master's right to interdict the slave, and a dropped right cannot be restored or qualified. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and contrasts it with the positions of Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar, who view permission as agency (tawkīl) and thus restrictable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The passage opens by stating that authorization is 'not suspended nor specified' (لا يتوقف ولا يتخصص). First, grasp what is being contested: can a master condition the permission by time, place, or type of trade? The Hanafi school denies such conditions are effective.
The matn's wording is decisive; the commentary explains it is according to us (عندنا).
2
Understand the Hanafi basis
The Hanafi position is that authorization is a dropping of the master's right (isqat). Since the slave acts with his own eligibility (أهلية نفسه), the master's right to interdict has been dropped. A dropped right does not return, and dropping (isqat) does not accept restrictions—analogous to divorce and manumission.
The analogy to divorce and manumission is key: those are absolute acts that cannot be conditioned.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Shafi'i and Zufar analogize authorization to agency (tawkīl/ إنابة). Agency, by nature, can be limited by the principal. So they argue that if the master specifies a type of trade, it is valid and the slave must comply.
Zufar is a Hanafi jurist who here agrees with Shafi'i, showing intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
4
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response distinguishes between isqat and wakalah. The master's authorization is not delegation of authority; it is removal of a barrier (the master's right). Once removed, the slave's natural capacity to trade is restored—unlike a judge's permission (إذن القاضي), which is true agency and can be restricted. Examples from the text: seller delivering goods with a condition on use—the condition is void; allowing hire to a specific person only—the restriction is ineffective.
The example of the judge's permission (إذن القاضي) is an exception: it is like agency, so it can be restricted. This sharpens the distinction.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a master says 'I authorize you to trade only in wheat,' in the Hanafi school the restriction is void; the slave can trade in anything. In the Shafi'i school, the restriction is binding and the slave limited to wheat.
This shows that the ikhtilaf directly affects the scope of the slave's authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : فلا يتوقف ولا يتخصص) يعني لا يتوقف بزمان ولا مكان ولا يتخصص بنوع من أنواع التجارة عندنا لما ذكرنا من تفسيره وقال الإمام الشافعي وزفر : هو عبارة عن توكيل وإنابة فينفذ عندهما ويتخصص، وعندهما يتصرف بأهلية نفسه وحق المولى قد أسقطه والساقط لا يعود كما إذا رضي المستأجران يؤجر عبده من شخص بعينه دون غيره والإسقاط لا يقبل التقييد دون غيره كالطلاق والعتاق ولو أسلم البائع المبيع إلى المشتري قبل نقد الثمن على أن يتصرف فيه نوعاً من التصرف دون غيره فإنه لا يعتبر تقييده لأنه إسقاط لحقه فلا يقبل التقييد بخلاف إذن القاضي فإنه بمنزلة الوكيل.
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'So it is not suspended nor specified.' Meaning, according to us, it is not suspended by time or place, nor specified to a type of trade, due to what we mentioned of its interpretation. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar said: It is an expression of delegation and deputyship, so according to them it is effective and can be specified. According to us, the slave acts with his own eligibility, and the master's right has been dropped, and that which is dropped does not return, just as when the two lessees consent that he may hire his slave to a specific person and not others; and dropping does not accept restriction like divorce and manumission. If the seller delivers the sold item to the buyer before payment of the price with the condition that he dispose of it in a certain type of disposition and not others, his restriction is not considered because it is dropping of his right, so it does not accept restriction, unlike the permission of the judge, for it is like a deputy.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The matn states that authorization (idhn) is not suspended (la yutawaqaf) and not specified (la yatakhassas). The commentator explains that it is not bound by time or place, nor restricted to a particular type of trade in the Hanafi school. This follows from the definition: authorization is a dropping of the master's right, and dropping is absolute. Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar (a Hanafi jurist who here agrees with Shafi'i) consider it a form of delegation (tawkīl). Therefore, they say it can be restricted. The Hanafi response emphasizes that the slave acts with his own eligibility (ahliyyah asliyyah) once the master's right is dropped; a dropped right does not return. They give an analogy: just as if a person drops a right (e.g., allowing hire to a specific person), the restriction is ineffective, and like divorce and manumission, which are not conditional. The same applies if a seller hands over the sold item with a condition about use; the condition is void because it is a dropping of right. However, the permission of a judge (idhn al-qadi) is different; it is like agency (wakalah) and can be restricted. This shows the Hanafi school's consistent application of the principle that isqat is absolute.

Key Terms
لا يتوقف ولا يتخصص (la yutawaqaf wa la yatakhassas): The authorization is not bound by time/place (la yutawaqaf) and not restricted to a specific type of transaction (la yatakhassas).
توكيل وإنابة (tawkīl wa inābah): Agency and deputyship; the view of Shafi'i and Zufar that authorization is a form of agency.
إسقاط الحق (isqat al-haqq): Dropping of a right; in this context, the master's right to interdict the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master says to his slave 'I authorize you to trade in wheat only, not in barley,' what is the ruling?
A) The restriction is valid; the slave may only trade in wheat.
B) The authorization is void entirely.
C) The restriction is invalid; the slave may trade in any goods.
D) It depends on whether the slave agrees.

Key Takeaway
A master's authorization to his slave is absolute; any attempt to restrict it is void in Hanafi law because it is a dropping of right, not a delegation of authority.

Leasing and Mudarabah with the Permitted Slave
Rulings on a ma'dhun engaging in ijara and mudarabah as part of trade.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which commercial contracts a ma'dhun may enter and distinguish between shirkat al-'inan and shirkat al-mufawadah.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun may lease and profit-share as part of trade, but his partnership is limited to 'inan, not mufawadah.

The ma'dhun (permitted slave) has the capacity to engage in trade activities. This passage outlines which commercial contracts are within his permission: leasing, mudarabah, sharecropping, hiring, and partnership. The key distinction is that he may form a shirkat al-'inan but not shirkat al-mufawadah because the latter involves suretyship, which is not covered by the general permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permitted acts
List all trade acts mentioned: mudarabah (both giving and taking), ijara (hiring out slaves and hiring freemen), muzara'ah (sharecropping), musaqah (irrigation contract), hiring houses and shops and renting them out, and shirkat al-'inan.
The text states each act is permissible because it is from the practice of merchants.
2
Note the basis for permissibility
The primary basis is 'urf (custom) of merchants. Additionally, a hadith is cited: 'The cultivator is the merchant of his Lord' to support muzara'ah.
The hadith is a zanni proof; it is not fully quoted but used as a supporting evidence.
3
Distinguish partnership types
Shirkat al-'inan involves mutual agency without suretyship; shirkat al-mufawadah includes both agency and suretyship. The ma'dhun may only enter the former because the latter requires kafalah (suretyship), which is beyond his permission.
If a mufawadah is formed, it is treated as 'inan to the extent of agency (wakalah) because 'mufawadah is 'inan plus an increase'.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
If a ma'dhun enters a contract that exceeds his permission (e.g., includes kafalah), the contract is valid only to the extent of what he possesses, i.e., agency. The portion involving suretyship is void.
This nuance preserves the validity of the trade where possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويستأجر ويضارب لأنه من صنيع التجار فيجوز له المضاربة أخذاً ودفعاً، وكذا الإجارة بأن يؤجر غلمانه ويستأجر أحراراً وله أن يدفع الأرض مزارعة ويأخذها ومساقاة لأن كل ذلك من عمل التجار قال عليه الصلاة السلام الزارع تاجر ربه» وله أن ويزرعه فيها ويستأجر البيوت والحوانيت ويؤجرها لما فيه من تحصيل المال ويشارك شركة عنان ولا يشارك مفاوضة لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن فلو فعلا ذلك كانت عناناً لأن المفاوضة عنان وزيادة فصحت بقدر ما يتملكه المأذون وهو الوكالة.
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): "And he leases and enters mudarabah because it is from the practice of merchants. So mudarabah is permissible for him, both giving and taking. Likewise leasing: he may hire out his slaves and hire freemen. He may give land for muzara'ah (sharecropping) and take it, and musaqah (irrigation contract), because all that is from the work of merchants. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'The cultivator is the merchant of his Lord.' And he may cultivate in it, and hire houses and shops and rent them out, for what that brings of acquiring wealth. And he may enter into a partnership of 'inan, but he may not enter into a partnership of mufawadah, because it is based on agency and surety, and surety is not included in the permission. So if they do that, it is 'inan, because mufawadah is 'inan plus an increase, so it is valid to the extent that the ma'dhun possesses, which is agency.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the permissibility of a ma'dhun engaging in ijara (leasing) and mudarabah (profit-sharing). The matn states it is allowed because it is part of merchants' practice. Mudarabah is allowed both as investor (rabb-ul-mal) and as worker (mudarib). Similarly, he can hire out his own slaves and hire free workers. He can enter into muzara'ah and musaqah contracts. The commentary quotes a hadith that the cultivator is a merchant. He can also hire properties and sublet. Regarding partnerships (shirkah), he can form a shirkat al-'inan (limited partnership) but not shirkat al-mufawadah (unlimited) because the latter involves kafalah (suretyship), which is not covered by the general permission. If they do form a mufawadah, it is treated as 'inan, valid to the extent of agency only.

Key Terms
المضاربة (al-mudarabah): A profit-sharing partnership where one provides capital and the other labor.
شركة العنان (shirkat al-'inan): A partnership where each contributes capital and work, but not including mutual suretyship.
شركة المفاوضة (shirkat al-mufawadah): A full partnership with agency and suretyship, requiring equal capital and capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a ma'dhun prohibited from entering a shirkat al-mufawadah?
A) Because it requires capital he does not have.
B) Because it involves kafalah (suretyship), which is not included in his permission.
C) Because it is invalid for all parties.
D) Because it requires the master's approval.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun may engage in all usual trade practices except those involving suretyship, such as shirkat al-mufawadah.

Can a Permitted Slave Hire Himself Out?
Ruling on a ma'dhun slave hiring himself out for labor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for permitting a ma'dhun slave to hire himself out and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun can hire himself out because permission to trade includes acquiring benefits; hiring is not selling oneself.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses whether a slave who has been granted permission to trade (ma'dhun) may hire himself out for labor. Imam al-Shafi'i objects, arguing that permission does not extend to contracts concerning one's own person. The Hanafi response clarifies that hiring oneself is a form of benefit acquisition, which falls within the scope of trade permission, unlike selling or pledging oneself which would negate that permission.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: the ma'dhun may hire himself out. This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
The matn says 'ويؤجر نفسه' — he hires himself.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Recognize Imam al-Shafi'i's objection: he says the ma'dhun cannot hire himself because the permission does not cover transactions on his own person.
Shafi'i analogizes to sale and pledge, which are clearly not permitted for the slave.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i reasoning: just as the slave cannot sell or pledge himself (because those would affect his person), hiring oneself should also be disallowed.
The analogy is based on the common element of 'contracts concerning the person'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis distinguish hiring from sale/pledge: hiring is a means of acquiring benefits (manafi'), which is included in the permission to trade. Sale would invalidate the permission because it transfers ownership of the slave; pledge would jeopardize the master's rights.
The key distinction: hiring does not nullify the permission; it is an act of trade.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, the ma'dhun can lawfully work for wages; in the Shafi'i school, he cannot, and any such contract would be invalid.
This affects the economic activity of ma'dhun slaves in different legal systems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ويؤجر نفسه) المأذون يؤجر نفسه وقد قدمناه . وقال الإمام الشافعي : لا يملك ذلك لأن الإذن لم يتناول التصرف في نفسه ولهذا لا يملك يبيع نفسه ولا أن يرهنها . قلنا الإذن يتضمن اكتساب المنافع والأجارة منه بخلاف البيع أو الرهن لأنه يبطل الإذن .
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): "And he hires himself." The ma'dhun hires himself out, and we have already mentioned it. Imam al-Shafi'i said: He does not have that right because the permission did not encompass transactions regarding his own person, and therefore he cannot sell himself or pledge himself. We said: The permission includes acquisition of benefits, and leasing from him is different from sale or pledge because that would invalidate the permission.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding whether a ma'dhun (permitted slave) can hire out his own labor (i.e., become an employee). The matn affirms he can, and the commentary explains the Hanafi reasoning. Imam Shafi'i argues that the master's permission for trade does not extend to contracts concerning the slave's own person (like selling himself or pledging himself). The Hanafi response: hiring oneself out is a means of acquiring benefits (manafi'), which is covered by the permission to trade. It is different from selling himself (which would terminate ownership) or pledging himself (which would jeopardize the master's rights). Thus, it is valid.

Key Terms
إجارة نفسه (ijarat nafsih): Hiring oneself out for labor in exchange for wages.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the text, why can a ma'dhun hire himself out but not sell himself?
A) Because selling requires the master's permission, but hiring does not.
B) Because hiring is a form of trade for benefits, while selling ends ownership.
C) Because Imam Shafi'i agrees with selling but not hiring.
D) Because the Quran explicitly permits hiring.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave may lawfully hire himself out in the Hanafi school because hiring is an acquisition of benefits, not a disposition of the person.

Usufructs of Usurped Property: Non-Liability
Presents the Hanafi ruling with a rebuttal of al-Shafi'i and an objection-resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the non-guarantee of usufructs in usurpation, explain the proof based on the nature of usufructs as accidents, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument and the istihsan exception.

Core Idea
Usufructs are not guaranteed in usurpation because they arise in the usurper's ownership.

This lesson tackles a key point of difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the liability for using a usurped property. The Hanafi school holds that the usurper is not liable for the usufructs derived from the property, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues for compensation. We will examine the reasoning behind each position, the handling of a potential objection from lease contracts, and the exceptional cases where istihsan imposes liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the clear ruling: the usurper does not guarantee the usufructs (manafi') of the usurped property, whether he uses them or they are destroyed.
This is the matn's ruling.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i argues the opposite: usufructs are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) and should be guaranteed just like physical assets, especially since they are guaranteed by contract in rental.
Opponent: al-Shafi'i
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: usufructs are mal mutaqawwim and are guaranteed by contract (e.g., rental), so by analogy they should be guaranteed in usurpation as well.
Key: qiyas on rental contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: usufructs are accidents (a'rad) that do not persist; they arise in the usurper's hand, becoming his property, and a person does not guarantee his own property. Also, destruction of usufructs is impossible as they perish instantly. The objection from rent is answered by explaining that rent is for the right to obtain usufructs from the owner, not for the usufructs themselves.
Key: distinction between the usufruct's existence and the right to it.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and the Istihsan Exception
Note the practical consequence: in Hanafi law, a usurper pays nothing for use; in Shafi'i, he must pay rent. Also, identify that the Hanafi school makes an exception based on istihsan for waqf, orphan's property, and property set for exploitation, where usufructs are guaranteed.
The exception proves the rule; it is based on juristic preference, not strict qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومنافع الغصب هذا معطوف على الحرة في قوله «ولا يضمن الغاصب منافع الغصب». وقال الشافعي يضمن منافع الغصب لأنها مال متقوم مضمون بالعقد كالأعيان ولنا أنها حصلت على ملك الغاصب فحدوثها في يده إذ هي لم تكن حادثة في يد المالك لأنها أعراض لا تبقى فيملكها دفعاً للحاجة والإنسان لا يضمن ملك نفسه. قال ابن قاضي زاده وهنا سؤال لم أر كثيراً من الشارحين تعرض له يقال لقائل أن يقول : مقتضى هذا الدليل أن لا تجب الأجرة على المستأجر فيما إذا حدت المنافع في يده كما في الاستئجار الدور والأراضي والدواب ونحوها لأن الإنسان كما لا يضمن ملكه لا يجب عليه الأجرة بمقابلة ملكه مع أنه تجب عليه الأجرة بالإجماع. وأجاب عنه في غاية البيان أن الأجرة عندنا لا تجب بمقابلة المنافع بل بالتمكن منها من جهة المالك وهذا السؤال ساقط من أصله لأن الغاصب يزعم حدوث المنافع على ملك نفسه، والمستأجر يعتقد حدوثها على ملك المؤجر فافترقا. وقوله بالإتلاف» متعلق أيضاً بالمنافع يعني وكذا منافع الغصب لا تضمن بالإتلاف لأنه لا يخلو إما أن يرد عليها الإتلاف قبل وجودها أو حال وجودها أو بعد وجودها وكل ذلك محال ؛ أما قبل وجودها فلأن إتلاف المعدوم لا يمكن، وأما حال وجودها فلأن الإتلاف إذا طرأ على الموجود رفعه فإذا قاربه منعه، وأما بعد وجودها فلأنها تنعدم كما وجدت فلا يتصور إتلاف المعدوم، ولأنا لو ضمناه المنافع لا يخلو إما أن يكون مضمونة بمثلها من المنافع لأنه لا قائل بذلك ولا بالدراهم لعدم الممثالة والمماثلة شرط في ضمان العدوان للآية قال صاحب العناية : واعترض بما إذا أتلف ما يتسارع إليه الفساد فإنه يضمنه بالدراهم وهي لا تماثله فدل على أن المماثلة ليست بمعتبرة لا يقال منافع الغصب مضمونة عندكم في الوقف ومال اليتيم وما كان معداً للاستغلال وهذا التعليل جارٍ فيه. قلنا : العلل على وفق القياس والقول بضمان المنافع فيما ذكر وجه الاستحسان.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And usufructs of usurpation). This is coordinated to 'the free woman' in his statement: 'And the usurper does not guarantee the usufructs of usurpation.' Al-Shafi'i said: He guarantees the usufructs of usurpation because they are valuable property guaranteed by contract, like physical assets. For us, they occurred in the ownership of the usurper, so they come into existence in his hand, as they were not occurring in the hand of the owner because they are accidents that do not persist, so he owns them to satisfy a need, and a person does not guarantee his own property. Ibn Qadizadeh said: Here there is a question that I have not seen many of the commentators address. It may be said: The implication of this proof is that rent would not be owed by the tenant when the usufructs occur in his hand, as in the lease of houses, lands, animals, etc., because just as a person does not guarantee his own property, he should not have to pay rent for his own property, yet rent is unanimously owed. The answer from Ghayat al-Bayan is that rent in our school is not owed for the usufructs themselves, but for the ability to obtain them from the owner's side. This question is fundamentally flawed because the usurper claims that the usufructs occur in his own ownership, while the tenant believes they occur in the ownership of the lessor, so they differ. His statement 'by destruction' also relates to usufructs, meaning likewise the usufructs of usurpation are not guaranteed by destruction because destruction cannot occur before their existence, at their existence, or after their existence. All is impossible: before existence because destroying the non-existent is impossible; during existence because destruction removes what exists, but if it coincides with existence it prevents it; after existence because they cease to exist as soon as they occur, so one cannot destroy the non-existent. Also, if we were to guarantee the usufructs, they would have to be guaranteed by similar usufructs—no one says that—or by dirhams, but there is no equivalence, and equivalence is a condition in tort liability according to the verse. Sahib al-Inaya said: An objection is made with the case of destroying something that quickly decays, where one compensates with dirhams even though they are not equivalent, showing that equivalence is not required. It cannot be said that usufructs of usurpation are guaranteed in your school in cases of waqf, orphan's property, and what is prepared for exploitation, and this reasoning applies there. We say: The legal causes are in accordance with analogy, and the ruling of guaranteeing usufructs in those mentioned is a matter of istihsan (juristic preference).
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the issue of usufructs (manafi') of a usurped property—whether the usurper must compensate the owner for the benefit derived from using the property. The matn states that the usurper does not guarantee usufructs. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that usufructs are valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that can be contracted (like in a rental), so they should be guaranteed like physical assets. The Hanafi position is based on the nature of usufructs: they are accidents (a'rad) that do not persist; they come into existence and perish at each moment. Since the usurper is in possession, the usufructs occur in his own hand, not the owner's, so they become his property (based on the principle of ownership through al-hiyazah for need). A person does not guarantee his own property. The commentator addresses a potential objection: if usufructs are not guaranteed, then why is rent obligatory in a lease? The answer is that rent is not for the usufruct itself but for the right to obtain them from the owner. Also, the scenarios are different: the usurper believes the usufructs are his, while the tenant knows they belong to the lessor. The passage then discusses the impossibility of destroying usufructs and the issue of equivalence in compensation. Finally, it addresses an exception: usufructs are guaranteed in cases of waqf, orphan's property, and property set for exploitation—but that is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not strict analogy. This shows the Hanafi commitment to systematic reasoning with flexibility where needed.

Key Terms
المنافع (al-manafi'): Usufructs; the beneficial uses derived from a property, such as living in a house or riding an animal.
المتقوم (al-mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is legally recognized and can be owned and traded.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from strict analogy for a preferred ruling due to a stronger evidence or need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on the usufructs of a usurped property?
A) The usurper must pay the rental value for the period of use.
B) The usurper is not liable for the usufructs because they occur in his own property.
C) The usurper is liable only if he destroys the property itself.
D) The usurper must pay the decrease in value of the property due to use.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, usufructs of usurped property are not guaranteed due to their non-persistent nature, with an istihsan-based exception for waqf, orphan's property, and commercial assets.

Muzara'ah Conditions: When All Produce Goes to One Party
Explaining the permissibility and conditions of muzara'ah where all produce goes to one party, with an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid from invalid conditions in muzara'ah contracts and explain the Hanafi reasoning treating them as ijara.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, a muzara'ah where all produce goes to one party is valid as ijara if the seed owner is the hirer.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines the permissibility of muzara'ah contracts where the entire produce is given to one party. While such an arrangement may seem unfair, the Hanafi school validates it under the category of ijara (hiring), with specific conditions on who owns seeds and hires whom. A key intra-Hanafi dispute with Isa ibn Abban is noted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main contract scenarios
List the combinations of who provides land, seeds, oxen, and work mentioned in the passage. Note the ruling for each.
The passage covers three main permutations plus the specific condition 'sow my land with your seeds, all produce for me'.
2
Determine the hukm and its basis
For each valid scenario, identify the hukm (ja'iz) and the legal basis: the contract is treated as ijara with the seed owner as hirer.
The worker's seeds become a loan (qard) if the landowner gets all produce, making the landowner the de facto seed owner.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare the majority view with Isa ibn Abban's opinion. Understand what Isa claimed and why the majority rejects it.
Isa ibn Abban argued that certain cases must be like the first (invalid), but the commentary implicitly overrules him.
4
Apply analogies from ijara
The passage uses analogies like hiring a tailor with his own needle. Explain how these support the ijara classification.
The oxen are considered subsidiary (tabi') to the worker as a tool.

كتاب المزارعة
الأرض لواحد والباقي لآخر أو يكون العمل لواحد والباقي لآخر فإن كانت الارض والبقر لواحد والعمل والبذر لآخر إن كان لأحدهما والباقي لآخر أو كان البذر والبقر للمزارع ازرع أرضي ببذرك على أن الخارج كله لي فهذا الشرط جائز ويصير العامل مقترضاً للبذر من رب الأرض ويكون العامل معيناً له. وفي العتابية : ازرع لي في أرضك ببذرك جاز ولو لم يقل لي والمسألة بحالها لم يجز. وقال عيسى بن أبان يجب أن يكون كالأول، ولو قال في المسئلة على أن الخارج نصفين جاز .
قال رحمه الله: (وإن تكون الأرض والبذر لواحد والعمل والبقر لآخر أو تكون الأرض لواحد والباقي لآخر أو يكون العمل لواحد والباقي لآخر) وهذه الجمل من جملة الشروط وإنما كان كذلك لأن من جوزها إنما جوزها على أنها إجازة ففي الصورة الأولى يكون صاحب البذر والأرض مستأجراً للعامل والبقر تبع له لاتحاد المنفعة لأن البقر آلة له فصار كمن استأجر خياطاً لخيطه له قميصاً بإبرة من عنده أو صباغاً ليصبغ له بصبغ من عنده ولآخر يقابل عمله دون الآلة له فيجوز والأصل فيها أن صاحب البذر هو المستأجر فتخرج المسائل على هذا كما رأيت وفي الصورة الثانية يكون صاحب البذر مستأجراً للعامل وحده بلا بقر بأجرة معلومة من الخارج فيجوز كما إذا استأجر خياطاً ليخيط له قميصاً بأجرة بإبرة من عند صاحب الثوب أو طناً أو بالنظير تمر له من المستأجر. قال في العتابية : الأصل أن المزارعة تنعقد إجارة وتتم شركة على منفعة الأرض والعامل، أما في الأرض فأثر عبد الله بن عمر وتعامل الناس وأما في العامل ففعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مع أهل خيبر وتعامل الناس .اهـ. وفي الفتاوى دفع الزرع المدرك مزارعة بالنصف للحفظ لا يجوز وفي غير المدرك يجوز ؛ كذا ذكره شيخ الاسلام خواهر زاده اهـ .
Book of Muzara'ah: Land for one and the rest for another, or work for one and the rest for another. If the land and oxen are for one, and work and seeds for another; if some for one and the rest for another; or if seeds and oxen for the muzari': 'Sow my land with your seeds on condition that all the produce is for me' – this condition is permissible, and the worker becomes a borrower of the seeds from the landlord, and the worker is an assistant for him. In al-Atabiyya: 'Sow for me in your land with your seeds' is permissible; if he did not say 'for me' and the case remains, it is not permissible. Isa ibn Abban said it must be like the first. And if he said in the case 'on condition that the produce is half and half', it is permissible. He (the author) said: (And if the land and seeds are for one, and work and oxen for another; or land for one and the rest for another; or work for one and the rest for another) – these sentences are among the conditions. And it is only so because those who permitted them permitted them as ijara. So in the first case, the owner of seeds and land hires the worker, and the oxen are subsidiary due to unity of benefit because the oxen are his tool; so it is like one who hires a tailor to sew a shirt for him with a needle from his own, or a dyer to dye with dye from his own; and for another, his work corresponds without the tool, so it is permissible. The principle is that the owner of seeds is the hirer, so cases come out on this as you have seen. In the second case, the owner of seeds hires the worker alone without oxen for a known wage from the produce, so it is permissible, as when one hires a tailor to sew a shirt for a wage with a needle from the owner of the cloth, or a turner, or for a similar date from the hirer. He said in al-Atabiyya: The principle is that muzara'ah is concluded as ijara and completed as a partnership on the benefit of land and worker. As for land, there is the athar of Abdullah ibn Umar and the practice of people; as for the worker, there is the action of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) with the people of Khaybar and the practice of people. End. And in al-Fatawa: Giving the ripened crop as muzara'ah in halves for preservation is not permissible; for unripened it is permissible. Thus mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam Khuwahar Zada. End.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on the conditions of validity in muzara'ah (sharecropping) contracts. It examines several permutations of who provides land, seeds, oxen, and labor. The core ruling is that if the landlord says, 'Sow my land with your seeds on condition that all produce is for me,' the contract is valid because the worker becomes a borrower (muqtarid) of the seeds, and the worker acts as an assistant (mu'in). However, if the produce is entirely for the worker, the contract is invalid unless the worker is considered a tenant or the arrangement is another valid type. The text notes a difference with Isa ibn Abban who equates certain cases. The commentary (sharh) explains that these contracts are treated as ijara (hiring), and the owner of seeds is the hirer. Oxen are subsidiary (tabi') to the worker if they serve as his tool. The hadith of the Prophet's dealing with Khaybar and the practice of Abdullah ibn Umar are cited as sources. The passage also references a fatwa that giving a ripe crop as muzara'ah for preservation is invalid, but for unripe crop it is valid. The author follows the Hanafi school, with Abu Hanifa's view as the basis, though Isa ibn Abban dissents.

Key Terms
المزارعة (al-muzara'ah): A sharecropping contract where land and labor are combined, with the produce shared as per agreement.
إجارة (ijara): A hire contract for services, considered analogous to the worker's role in muzara'ah.
تبع (tabi'): Subsidiary or accessory; used to indicate that oxen are subsidiary to the worker or land in the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the landlord says 'Sow my land with your seeds on condition that all produce is for me,' the contract is valid because:
A) The worker becomes a borrower of the seeds and an assistant.
B) The worker becomes a partner in the land.
C) The landlord is considered the worker's agent.
D) The contract becomes a sale of seeds.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a muzara'ah contract where all produce goes to one party is valid as a hiring (ijara) if the seed owner is the hirer and the worker's seeds are considered a loan.

No Produce, No Wage: The Core Ruling of Muzara'ah
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq: ruling that no produce yields no wage for the worker

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the worker gets nothing if no produce emerges in a valid muzara'ah contract, using the Hanafi analysis of the contract as either a lease or partnership.

Core Idea
In a valid muzara'ah, the worker's right is to a share of produce—no produce means no wage.

This lesson unpacks one of the foundational rulings of muzara'ah (sharecropping): if the land yields no produce, the worker is entitled to nothing. The Hanafi school bases this on the nature of the contract—whether it is considered a lease (ijarah) or a partnership (shirkah). We will see how the classical scholars reasoned from this premise and addressed an objection from al-Inayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type
Recognize that muzara'ah is treated by Hanafi jurists as either a lease (ijarah) where the wage is the agreed share of produce, or a partnership (shirkah) where both parties share in the actual output.
2
Apply the rule for each type
If it is a lease, the wage is the specified share; if no produce exists, the wage is nonexistent, so nothing is owed. If it is a partnership, the partnership exists only in the produce; if no produce, there is no partnership profit for the worker.
3
Contrast with a corrupt contract
The rule differs if the contract is corrupt (fasid): if the seed owner's contract is corrupt, the worker may be entitled to an equivalent wage even without produce.
4
Analyze the objection from al-Inayah
Study the analogy with hired land that is destroyed before delivery, and the Hanafi response that the wage here was lost after delivery (by possession of the seed). Evaluate whether the counterobjection is valid.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position
Summarize why the worker gets nothing: the wage or share is contingent on the existence of produce, which never materialized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يزرعها حنطة فالخارج كذا، وإن زرعها شعيراً فكذا وإن زرعها سمسماً فكذا فهذا على أربعة أوجه : أما إن قال ازرعها أو زرعت فيها أو زرعت منها أو زرعت بعضاً منها فالمزارعة في الأولين جائزة لأنه خيره بين والرضا، وفي رواية كتاب المزارعة لا يحتاج إلى القضاء أو الرضا اهـ. ولو مات
Book of Muzara'ah. So the produce is according to the condition, and if nothing comes out then nothing for the worker. And whoever refuses to proceed is compelled except for the straw? It is valid because it is the intended purpose, and the straw is half each. And if they stipulate the grain half and the figs for the owner of the land, it is valid because it is a condition that does not contradict the contract because it is the growth of his property. And if they stipulate the straw for the worker, it is corrupt because it is a condition contrary to the requirement of the contract, and maybe it leads to cutting the partnership by a blight afflicting the crop so the grain does not form and only straw comes out. He said: And the tithe is upon both according to them, and according to Imam Abu Hanifa upon the owner of the land. If the imam does not take the tithe, then it belongs to the owner of the land according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and according to them it belongs to both. And if the owner of the land says to the worker: I do not know what the imam will take, the tithe or half, because the half is for me after he takes, it is valid according to them. Likewise in al-Muhit. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If it is valid, then the produce is according to the condition due to the validity of the commitment.) He said in al-Muhit: As for increase and decrease in muzara'ah and mu'amalah, the best is if the contracted thing is in a state that allows the initiation of muzara'ah and mu'amalah, then increase in them is permissible. And if one of them gives the other in the produce, if the increase is before harvest and the crop has reached its full growth, increase is permissible because the initiation of the contract is permissible as long as it is capable of increase, otherwise not. And decrease is permissible in both cases, the case of accepting increase and after it, because it is a relinquishment. And if he sells the land given in muzara'ah or mu'amalah, the sale is suspended pending the permission of the muzari' and the worker. If he permits, it remains until the end of the muzara'ah and mu'amalah, and the buyer is given the choice: if he wishes he waits or rescinds. The author did not address when the contract is contingent or conditioned, and we will explain that. He said, and also in it: If he gives the land and seed for a year on condition that he cultivates it without plowing, then for the worker a quarter of the produce; if he plows it, then a third; if he plows and builds, then half; whatever they stipulate is valid. Likewise if the seed is from the muzari'. The second section: giving the land on condition that he cultivates it with wheat, then the produce is such; if he cultivates it with barley, then such; if with sesame, then such. So this is in four aspects: If he says 'cultivate it' or 'you cultivated in it' or 'you cultivated from it' or 'you cultivated some of it', then the muzara'ah in the first two is valid because he gave him a choice between three contracts. If he cultivates something from the three varieties, then the produce is according to what they stipulated. And if he says 'what you cultivated from it' or 'some of it', then the muzara'ah is corrupt because if he cultivates some wheat, some barley, and some sesame, then that part is unknown. And if the seed is from the worker and he stipulates that if he cultivates it with wheat then between them half, and if he cultivates it with barley then that is for the worker, it is valid by istihsan. The first is muzara'ah and the second is loan of land. Then Muhammad mentioned choice among three, but did not mention whether choice is permissible in more than that? Hisham narrated that it is not permissible. Third section: giving the land on condition that he cultivate it with his seed in the first of Jumada al-Ula, then the produce is half, and if he delays then a third for the muzari'. Both conditions are valid according to them, and the explanation of the proof is sought therein. End. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (If nothing comes out, then nothing for the worker) because it is either a lease or a partnership. If it is a lease, then the obligatory in a valid contract is the specified [wage], and it is nonexistent so he is not entitled to anything else. If it is a partnership, then the partnership is in the produce only, so he is not entitled to anything else, unlike when the muzara'ah is corrupt and [the seed owner?] and it becomes void by the death of one of them. If the period passes and the crop has not ripened, then the cultivator must pay an equivalent wage for the land where the equivalent wage is due for the period, and the lack of produce does not prevent its obligation. He said in al-Inayah: And it is problematic with one who hires a specific piece of land, then the hired person does the work and the land is destroyed before delivery, then he must pay the equivalent rent. So this should be similar because the muzara'ah was valid and the specified wage was lost. And it was answered that here the wage was lost after delivery because the muzari' took possession of the seed from which the produce branches, and possession of the origin is possession of its branches. And the other specific [wage] is not owed, so here too. And one may say: This answer is not correct in the case of hiring land, because the owner of the land does not take possession of the seed from which the produce branches so that his possession is possession of its branches. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever refuses to proceed is compelled except the owner of the seed) because it is concluded as a lease, and lease is a binding contract, but it is dissolved due to an excuse. If the owner of the seed refuses to proceed, he is excused because he cannot proceed except by destroying his property, which is throwing the seed on the land, and he does not know if it will come out or not, so it becomes similar to if he hired him to demolish his house and then refused. And if the worker refuses, he is compelled to work. And if the owner of the seed and the land refuses after he has plowed the land, then there is nothing for him for the work of plowing in the court because his work is only valued by the contract, and he has forfeited it by [taking] a part of the produce, and there is no produce, and he must in what is between him and Allah the Exalted pay an equivalent wage for it, so that he is not deceived by him. Because he is harmed by it and he is repelled, so he is content by paying him his equivalent wage. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it becomes void by the death of one of them) because it is a lease, and a lease becomes void by the death of one of the contracting parties if he contracted for himself, and we have explained it in the chapter of Ijarah. This absolute statement is the answer of qiyas, and by istihsan, if he dies and the crop has grown, the lease contract remains until the crop is harvested, then it becomes void in the remainder. Because in keeping it for this period is consideration of both rights. So the worker or his heir works accordingly, and when harvested, they divide according to what they stipulated, and there is no need in the remainder. And if the owner of the land dies before planting after plowing and digging canals, the muzara'ah is rescinded because there is no destruction of the worker's property, and nothing for the worker in return for the work because it is valued by the produce and there is no produce. Nothing is owed, unlike the first case where he is compensated because he was deceived by his choice. And if the owner of the land has a debt and cannot pay it except by selling the land, then the muzara'ah is rescinded before planting and the land is sold for the debt, and nothing is owed to the worker for plowing and digging canals. And if the crop has grown but not harvested, the land is not sold for the debt until the crop is harvested because in selling there is nullification of the muzari's right, and delay is easier than nullification. And the judge releases him from prison if he was imprisoned for it because he did not prevent the sale of the land, he was not a dilatory, and imprisonment is the penalty for dilatoriness. And in al-Dhakhirah: If the owner of the land dies after planting but before growth, does the muzara'ah remain? Some said it remains. Some said it does not remain, so it is rescinded. And in it also: Is the rescission of muzara'ah in need of the judge's decree? It was said: And in the narration of al-Ziyadat, it needs the judge's decree and consent, and in the narration of the book of muzara'ah, it does not need the judge's decree or consent. End. And if he dies.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the rulings of muzara'ah (sharecropping) from Kanz al-Daqaiq. The author, Imam al-Mawsili, presents the matn statements and then expands them with reasoning, citations from other works (al-Muhit, al-Inayah, al-Dhakhirah), and intra-Hanafi discussions. The passage covers three main rulings: (1) distribution of produce and the condition that if nothing comes out, the worker gets nothing; (2) compulsion to proceed with the contract except for the owner of the seed; (3) termination of the contract by death of a party. The commentary uses qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) to derive and refine the rulings. For example, in the first ruling, the author explains that the contract is either a lease (ijara) or a partnership (shirkah), so if no produce comes out, the worker is not entitled to anything because the specified wage (in case of lease) is non-existent, or partnership is only in produce. There is an objection from al-Inayah comparing it to hiring land where the land is destroyed before delivery, but the author defends the ruling by arguing that the wage here is lost after delivery because the seed was taken possession of. In the second ruling, the owner of the seed is excused from being compelled because he cannot proceed without risking his property. The worker is compelled to work. If the owner of both seed and land refuses after plowing, he is not liable in court but must pay an equivalent wage in conscience to avoid deception. The third ruling states that the contract becomes void by death of either party by qiyas, but by istihsan, if the crop has grown, the contract remains until harvest to protect both parties' rights. The passage also includes details about increase and decrease in the contract, different scenarios of specifying crops, and the effect of debt on the land. The overall authority weight is from the commentaries of the later Hanafi scholars (al-Mawsili, etc.), building on the zahir al-riwayah of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms
المزارعة (al-muzāraʿah): A contract of sharecropping where one party provides land and the other works, sharing the produce according to agreement.
الخارج (al-khārij): The produce or output from the land in a muzara'ah contract.
الاجارة (al-ijārah): A lease contract where one party provides a benefit (e.g., land) in exchange for a wage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid muzara'ah contract, if the land produces no crop due to natural causes, what is the legal position for the worker?
A) The worker is entitled to an equivalent wage for his labor.
B) The worker gets nothing because his compensation is tied to the produce.
C) The worker gets half the value of the seed if he provided it.
D) The worker gets a quarter of the land as compensation.

Key Takeaway
In a valid Hanafi muzara'ah, the worker's compensation is contingent on actual produce; without produce, the worker receives nothing.

Ijarah — Hire and Lease
intermediate
8 min
Compulsion and the Exception of the Seed Owner in Muzara'ah
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq: rulings on who is compelled to proceed in muzara'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who is compelled to proceed in a muzara'ah contract and who is excused, and state the compensation rules for early termination.

Core Idea
The seed owner is excused from compulsion because he risks his property; the worker must work, and a refusal after plowing may require conscience compensation.

This lesson covers the compulsion (ijbar) aspects of muzara'ah. Once the contract is valid, each party generally must fulfill it. However, the owner of the seed has a special excuse: he cannot be forced to proceed because that would risk losing his seed without certainty of return. The worker, on the other hand, is compelled to work. We also explore what happens if the seed owner also owns the land and refuses after plowing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of compulsion
Since muzara'ah is a binding contract (like ijarah), both parties are normally compelled to execute it.
2
Recognize the exception for the seed owner
The seed owner is excused because proceeding requires him to sow his seed, which may be lost if no produce emerges. This is treated as an excuse (udhr).
Analogy: hiring someone to demolish a house then backing out.
3
Apply the rule to the worker
The worker is compelled to perform the labor; he cannot back out without cause.
4
Analyze the case of refusal after plowing
If the owner of both seed and land refuses after plowing, he is not held liable in court for the plowing work (since no produce exists to value the work), but he must pay an equivalent wage in conscience (baynahu wa bayna Allah) to avoid being a source of deception.
5
Distinguish court liability from conscience liability
The difference is crucial: court liability requires a legal basis (here, the work is valued by the contract which is forfeited), while conscience liability is a moral/religious obligation to prevent unfairness.
